SATURDAY, DECEMBER 12, 1891.
A Question for the N.S.W. Labour
Party.
Every man has his price except the man
who has the price. In other words, no man
is to bo so much pitied as the man who
places his supreme good in the present
enjoyment of so much worldly gear. Every
man has his price, but the price may not be
in the shape of money. There is some one
thing around which the hopes and aspira-
tions of most men cling - it may be ease,
competence, leisure, honour, love, retire-
ment, what-not. One man alone in the
world is so wise that he is an eternal fool.
He has the price of everybody else—money.
He doesn’t want for anything, because he
holds in his cheque-book the sum of human
endeavour—the acme of human desire.
Alexander wept when he had no fresh
worlds to conquer. So the mere man
of money is necessarily supremely un-
happy, because nobody holds the' talis-
man by which his inmost nonentity of a
soul can be conjured. Building so low,
he never lifts his ediGce above the founda-
tion, and, what is also a very sad thing for
him, he has a supreme contempt for all
buildings not reared over a
bank-vault.
When money is the touch-stone of wisdom
everybody hastens to be wise. It is more or
less felt, even by such of the Labour-mem-
bers in the New South Wales Parliament
who have just begun to blossom into tailor-
cut clothes, that poverty is a sad thing, a
reprehensible thing ; which, if not exactly
criminal, is at least hardly respectable.
A little while ago, in the first democratic
flush of an election-freshening of blood,
the “one-man-one-vote'’ principle lifted
its head quite boldly, and it was rather
rashly advanced that a pauper even might
be a man, not sunk so low as to demand
either clergyman’s or medico’s attestation to
the fact. To-day a change has come over the
dream of Caesar. The soldier and the police-
man are each to have a vote ; the “pauper”
is not to have a vote. A soldier is a man,
even a policeman might be considered one ;
but a pauper is only the astral phantom of a
man that has been. And mark ye ! the New
South Wales Parliament once in a sublime
rage of occult forgetfulness presented the
astral phantom of a man “ that had been ”
with £lO,OOO, in memoriam of his past
services to the State.
* * *
New South Wales has just enfranchised
the soldier—the non-individual item whose
sole right is a right to do as he is told ; the
trigger-pulling machine who may to-morrow
be ordered to shoot down the people who
pay his wages, on that occasion when they
demand a little more money themselves
wherewith to pay wages—to soldiers, police-
men and judges, ay, and hangmen; for
under a democratic and representative sys-
tem the citizen is compelled to buy the rope
which helps him into another, and perhaps a
better, world. Many a man who speaks
loudly to-day anent a pauper-vote will in a
few years be consigned to that benevolent
asylum whose rise and progress is one of the
glories of our glorious land. No matter !
The pauper is out of favour, and the
Pharaoh of party who knew and recognised
him once now knows him no more for ever,
Mr. Garvan has disowned him, and Mr.
Reid alludes to his advocacy of an asylum
vote as a sportive incident of infinite jest.
* # •
In a few weeks it is just possible that Sir
Henry Parkes will be “ placated ” out of
the public Treasury for his relinquishment
of the various things which he was power-
less to control. His position, in such a
case, will be absolutely analogous to that of
a pensioner at Liverpool Asylum, at which
the partial services of one doctor, enjoying
private practice, are distributed among 900
men. Will the Parliament of the province
put him on a political shelf thercfoi’e, and
deprive him of all power to write, vote,
and speak in any political sense whatsoever?
Sir John Robertson was a political some-
body up to the day of his death, although he
had accepted the alms of the nation.
W herefore, then, should the pauper be dis-
franchised? Say he is old, feeble and
outworn. Good ! Place the franchise
on a mentally-competitive basis, and
score every name whose owner cannot
answer certain easy questions in that
science of politics to which Parkes has failed
all his life to give one satisfactory solu-
tion. In the first instance, compel every
member of the Legislative Council to show
cause why he should not be disfranchised on
the ground of mental incapacity. This
scheme may not be possible, but it is logical 1
* # *
An empty pocket is the worst of crimes,
and every man possessing, or expecting to
possess, money-power (which in New South
Wales is, unfortunately, political power also)
is puzzled to know why the pauper should
have a vote. The pauper has forfeited his
vote,, say they ;heis a State pensioner. Is
a Judge not a State pensioner—in the sense-
of having his daily bread assured by the
State beyond the possibility of competition
or a soldier, or a policeman, or a civil
servant ? The inmate of the Benevolent
Asylum has gone down at the end of a fight
in which one of the conditions of struggle
was free competition. The Civil servant^is
protected from this. He serves at a stated
wage, with the prospect of a progressive
rise, and when he is superannuated he walks
out of his office a State pensioner —with a
vote. The Civil Service is a national institu-
tion. True, but it belongs to us, and as
only a limited number of us can serve as
Civil servants, with the prospect of a pension
and the accompanying retention of the
franchise, others of us are compelled at
our life’s decline to have recourse to the
Benevolent Asylum—for the effort to sup-
port the Civil Service is bound to bear
heavily on some shoulders—and we have a
right to claim admission ; for we pay for
benevolent agylums while we are able to
pay. They belong to us, as citizens of a
free and freely-taxed nation, as much as do
the pictures in the National Gallery, and the
inmate of the benevolent asylum is no more
a State pauper than the Civil Service pen-
sioner is a State pauper.
* # #
Pauperism is, by the attempt to disfran-
chise it, placed by the New South Wales
Parliament on a level with lunacy and crime.
If every “ man ” is to have a vote make not
the test of manhood a property qualification.
The newly-enfranchised soldier is a State-
paid machine doing certain work for which,
if he serves long enough, ho will ultimately
receive a pension. Wherein does he differ,
then, from the citizen whose life has been
spent in supporting the machinery of State
amid a severe environment of free compe-
tition, and who, failing at last, receives also
a pension—a meed of assurance for the
future for the efforts of the past ? We are
sorry that the Labour-party lias seen lit to
abandon their stand in regard to the pauper
franchise. It was just; it was logical. The
only reason possible to be urged against the
clause was that of feeble-mindeduess ; but
how absurd this is ! A man is not necessarily
feeble-minded because he is poor. We would
never deprive a wealthy man of the franchise
on such a ground. We seldom deprive a
wealthy man of the right to bequeath pro-
perty (in the case of the members of the
Legislative Council, to make laws) merely
because he is feeble-minded.
* * *
Lot us be honest. The objection to the
enfranchisement of the paupers is a money
objection, and nothing else ; and all objec-
tions to the “One-man-one-vote” principle
are likewise money objections. They are all
simply successive attempts to destroy the
integrity of the measure. “ Would you give
the franchise to soldiers, to prisoners, to
policemen, to paupers ?” Pshaw ! what do
all such queries amount to ? We would
confer the franchise upon all men ; “ One-
man-one-voto ” to every person capable of
using it, the right to the exercise of such fran-
chise being voided only by lunacy or proven
crime. All the exceptions we have heard
raised are ridiculous, because they involve
a discussion on each occasion as to what we
mean by “a man ! ” The Bulletin has
supported the One-man-one-vote principle in
its entirety and in its integrity in the past,
and it intends to support it consistently in
the future within the fixed definitiveness of
the English word “ man," which includes
we have reason to believe—“pauper.” Why
there should be anything ludicrous about a
pauper exercising the franchise we are unable
to surmise. To us a “ pauper ”is simply a
“man” who has paid his share in the past
to the institutions of his country, and who,
when he has been worsted in the struggle for
existence, has a right to boldly walk up to
the front-door of the Benevolent Asylum and
to demand admission in the name of his mis-
fortunes, without forfeiting the right to vote,
or any other right which appertains to him
as a “ man” and a citizen.
Twopence an Hour.
The scheme for the introduction of cheap
Italian labour into Queensland is prospering.
The first shipment of workers has either already
landed or is close at hand, and an indefinite
number of further shipments are coming close
behind. The Italian Government has bestowed
its blessing on the project, for Italy is living
on the two horns of a dilemma, and there are
many dangerous elements in that country, or
elements that are likely to become dangerous,
which the men in authority would gladly give
away to any nation that will take them. The
tremendous effort to make Italy a Great Power,
whereas Providence intended her for a' very
small one, has loaded the country with taxes,
until the Italian landholder, in many cases,
simply abandons the land which has become a
dead loss on his hands, and leaves it to the
Crown, or the foxes, or the kites and crows, or
to anyone who wants to take a ruinous contract
and die over it. The frantic desire to make the
modern Italian look like the resurrected
Roman, and to scream loudly in the councils of
Europe, has burdened the nation with a huge
army that never won a battle, and a ponderous
navy that only once went into action and then
ran away. There are public buildings of
marble scattered all over the peninsula, and
splendid highways have been opened up,
and fortifications of a stupendous order are
everywhere, and new railways have been con-
structed in all directions, and the unshod
Italian taxpayer meanwhile roosts in a kennel
with the dogs and the goats. The Government
endeavours to gild the mud, and to fresco the
filth and the vermin, and it has put a
varnish over the ignorance and the leprosy and
the sores and plague-spots of this foul and
rotten land, where all the nation is famishing in
order that the kingdom may grow palaces.
Italy has invented new and horrible diseases
born of the strange and loathsome food on which
the peasantry eke out a miserable subsistence,
and when the Italian leper is in the last stages
of his complaint he can always get on the lee
side of a pompous cathedral that cost half-a-
million pounds to erect, and there die comfort-
ably. There are mysterious plagues always
floating about the slums of the southern cities,
and unspeakable ignorance is everywhere, and
every third man is either a soldier or a priest or
a tax-gatherer, and the murder-rate is the high-
est in the world, and everywhere there are gold
images and dead goats, and silver, and marble,
and festering garbage, and nakedness, and mud-
hovels, and wretchedness that is past all possi-
bility of expression.
* # *
Therefore, it has occurred to the Queensland
capitalist, and to the Coalition which is his
prophet, that the hog-like illiteracy, the
poverty, the slavishness, and the strange
diseases of Italy are matters that may be turned
into sources of great profit. The object of the
Queensland planter is, first of all, to get cheap
labour, and the Italian is cheap—there are few
cheaper men on earth than the hungry, sun-
blackened peasant of Naples or Tuscany, or the
agriculturist who lives on brown crusts and
fruit among the Apennines. Then the im-
ported labourer must be industrious, and
the Italian, who labours night and day
to support that bloated armament which
always bolts on the day of battle, and that
royal family which is just a shade more moral
than the Ptolemies, inasmuch as it does
actually draw the line at the intermarriage of
brothers and sisters,- though it draws it
nowhere else—fills the bill through sheer force
of necessity. And lastly, the Italian is par-
ticularly suited to the situation by reason of an
ignorance which is within an ace of that of the
camel or the hyena; and, therefore, he freely
signs a contract by which he binds himself
to years oi servitude, at infinitesimal wages,
long hours, the food of a hog, and the privilege
of sleeping in any hole or hovel which may
come handy. The consignments of Romans and
Piedmontese who are now en route for Australia
are pledged to work for a long term at the rate
of 12 hours per day for a cash remuneration of
12s. per week —or exactly twopence per hour.
During the term of his engagement the
imported chattel will be an abject slave in the
most slavish sense of the term. If he leaves
his work he can be put in gaol for breach of
contract. If he absents himself in order to
appeal to the law—provided there is any law
within walking-distance against the pro-
ceedings of his master he can be gaoled
for quitting his legal employment contrary
to the Act of Ceorge IV. or some
similar individual. The civil law can only
be appealed to within certain hours, and a
degraded Italian who works from dawn till
after sunset must necessarily break the law by
leaving his employment before he can appeal
against anybody else’s breach of law. In his
ignorance of the manners, language and
customs of the country, he will be practically
at the mercy of his boss and patron, and, in
short, he will be so nearly a duplicate of the
old Louisiana nigger that the difference between
the two will not be worth swearing at.
* # #
The new Italian will not become in any sense
or by any possibility an Australian. Un-
less the scheme of wholesale immigration is
cut short by violence there will present!}' groAV
up in the North a great Italian community
with its own language, manners and customs,
its own squalor, ignorance, superstition, and
other disadvantages. It will be located in
remote regions where education is scarce, and
the dirty young Italian will simply grow up to
be the image and reproduction of the dirty old
Italian who went before him. There will be no
progress in a knowledge of the decencies or
comforts of life ; progress of this kind is utterly
incompatible with extreme poverty, and the
family that lives in a hovel amid a tropical
swamp on an income of 12s. a week never does
progress. When each batch of labourers get to
the end of their term of servitude they will
probably move South, and form a little squalid
Italy of their own in the outlying parts
of the coastal cities. They are not likely to
re-engage themselves for a further term on the
same unspeakable terms as before, for nothing
short of absolute mental incapacity can prevent
them acquiring some knowledge of the vast-
ness of the swindle by which they were in-
duced to work for 12 hours a day and
twopence an hour, in a land where Is. per hour
is regarded as a reasonable wage. Their
masters are not likely to re-engage them on
equitable terms, because, so long as the
importation of indented serfs is permitted
by law, and so long as Russia, Italy,
Portugal, China, India, and similar lands can
supply cheap pauper labour in indefinite
quantities, he can always find new workers to
grovel in his cane-fields at twopence per hour,
and ask no questions. And the Italian cannot
be shunted home again against his will merely
because his owner is done with him ; he is the
tattered representative of a nation which
commands some 2,000,000 armed men, and a
huge ironclad fleet, and behind it is the force
and the moral influence of the great Triple
Alliance, which is a power that the British
empire cannot disregard. Therefore, if the
Italian desires to stay he probably will do so,
and the chances are that he will desire to stay,
for no poor man in his senses ever wants to go
back to Italy. And being in a state of chronic
insurrection against the 12 hours a day and the
12s. a week, his ambition will probably be
to work only 10 hours a day and earn 245.
a week. Therefore, the native Queenslander
must either, through pressure of competition,
come down to the Italian standard, and
qualify himself for doing so by acquiring
the Italian’s dirt and ignorance, and the
dull animal contentment which enables him
to enjoy his dirt and his ignorance—or
else he must elevate him, by some violent pro-
cess, to the level of the country, and then he
must somehow find employment for him to
prevent him sinking again, through pressure of
poverty, after he is elevated. As Queensland
is already reduced to the lowest stage of misery
through trade depression, want of employment,
and the competition of incessant hordes of mis-
cellaneous cheap immigrants, imported at the
nation’s expense by the infamous Coalition, the
latter possibility is exceedingly remote. And,
even granting that it could be accomplished,
there could be no end to the process. Half
Europe and Asia is ready to pour into Aus-
tralia by contract, even on the terms offered by
the Queensland planters, and the attempt to
elevate these hordes to the industrial standard
of the country is just the same as attempting
to pump a vessel dry with the whole Pacific
ocean coming through the bottom.
* * #
The Italian, however, would have other uses
besides his ostensible employment of hoeing
sugar-cane in a sultry land at twopence per
hour. At the present time the Coalition is
busily engaged in establishing itself as a
despotism of one fat man and one thin one, by
the simple process of striking off the roll nearly
everybody who can’t be relied upon to vote the
Coalition ticket at next election. If the experi-
ment is successful the next Parliament will con-
sist solely of a bigoted assortment of mono-
polists and landlords whose one business
will be to shore up the interests of Property.
Then, having got in by “faking” the electoral
rolls by ingenious but iqiquitous means within
the law, they will probably abolish man-
hood suffrage by formal process of law, so as to
save the necessity for such dangerous experi-
ments in the future, and establish the suffrage
on a solid property basis, and altogether extin-
guish the working-man as a political factor.
This is assuredly what the Coalition will do if
circumstances are favourable, and its courage
holds out—it possesses all the unscrupulousness
and all the ruffianism which are essential for
the work, but its pluck and its mental capacity
are doubtful. But if this scheme should fall
through, and the existing suffrage continues in
force, the imported Italian will prove a valu-


.j-jle force in the political world. He can be
shunted on the roll wholesale ; he will be guilt-
less of any knowledge of the English language ;
a iid he will know absolutely nothing of any
local matters beyond his 12 hours per diem and
pis 12s. a week. He is not likely to learn
English, because he will live in a colony of his
oWU countrymen ; and he is certain not to learn
politics, for newspapers will be to him an un-
intelligible thing, and political propagandists—
except those of the variety approved by his
p oS s and owner —will be warned otF under the
law of trespass. Therefore, the Italian serf
will be eminently fitted to give a block vote in
t l, e interests of Property. He will probably
„ e t a holiday and a choice drunk as an induce-
ment to vote as his proprietor tells him to ;
and as an election will be a pleasant break in
pPe dire and horrible monotony of hoeing sugar-
cane for 72 hours a week, he will be an emi-
nently reliable voter, and a solid influence in
shaping the future destinies of Queensland.
* * #
The Italian immigrant —if he is permitted to
increase and multiply at the pleasure of the
Coalition —will supply an interesting problem
for the future. In the first place, he will doubt-
less introduce those quaint and curious secret
societies which have done so much to help along
the rate of mortality in Italy, and which have
been transplanted with so much success to the
shores of the Gulf of Mexico. For wherever a
few Italians are gathered together there a secret
society will be found which exists for purposes
of blackmail, and for the supply of corpses to
the local cemetery. It sends out its anonymous
ultimatum to everybody who has offended or in-
iuredanltalian, and if the ultimatum isofnoavail
it is followed up by prompt assassination.
—if it will bear transplantation—the com-
jjjo 1 pauper will doubtless bring with him the
pellagra —tlirrt curious Italian leprosy of which
he has the copyright, and which is a complaint
eminently fitted for use in the far North. And
along with it he will fetch along his great and
comprehensive dislike to all forms of progress,
his venerable and hoary superstitions, and that
valuable military tendency which leads him—
when his country is in peril—to go into battle
whooping a little in the rear, and to come out
of it yelling a little in front. Furthermore, he
will doubtless bring out one or two other
characteristics unnecessary to describe.
# # #
The Bulletin views this new immigrant
from a purely Australian —in other words, from
a purely selfish—standpoint. Everybody in
Australia and everybody elsewhere views him
and every other immigrant from exactly the
same standpoint, but very few are prepared to
admit the fact —which makes all the difference.
The Italian in his own land is a wretched, im-
poverished being, who is loaded down with
military pomp, and fast dying of hunger in
order that his Government may cover the land
With gigantic fortifications ancf own the largest
army—in proportion to population —that the
earth has ever seen. Even a life on the
Queensland plantations might possibly be a
glimpse of Paradise to many a peasant from the
sun-blasted Campagna or the plains of Lombardy,
and if this country refuses him a refuge, the
curse will be loud and long in the Christian
land of England, and many British newspapers
will wail over the selfishness of the Australian
nation. But all over the world every sym-
pathetic nation desires to aid the poor and the
oppressed in the same old way, by sending
them to be a nuisance and a burden to some
other nation, and every nation weeps tears of
desolation when the other nation declines to
take them in. The Italian immigrant will
make wages lower and the struggle of life
harder for the Australian ; and he may easily
become a serious danger to political freedom,
and, therefore, the Australian people will pre-
sently class him with the Chinaman and the
rabbit-pest, and act accordingly. There is
doubtless something inhuman in this policy,
but there is nothing in it more inhuman than
usual; every nation and every Government
looks after itself alone, and it is for that pur-
pose only that they exist. This is the one
fixed principle that is recognised everywhere on
earth, but from force of ancient habit it is con-
sidered a commendable thing to snuffle and
enter a protest when any other State puts the
principle into practice ; and when the State
that entered the protest does the same thing in
its turn, then the other State snuffles instead ;
and thus matters are made about square all
round. Only, as this ancient formula is grow-
ing very wearisome, the sympathetic aspect of
the case has been omitted in this article. The
Englishman, when he extends the hand of
friendship to any down-trodden foreigner,
generally bids him go to Australia and be at
rest. If the Australian briefly states that he
hasn’t time to care whether any particular
individual—save himself—is at rest or not, the
fact may not look so well, but it comes to the
same thing in the end.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Scream.
The following letter has arrived at this
office:—
Editor Bulletin.—Sir, —I am instructed by the Central
Committee of the Labour Electoral League of N.S.W. to
take the opportunity to deny the unwarrantable and
untruthful assertions contained in article published in
The Bulletin of the 21st November. From your remarks
pu that occasion it would appear that you are lamentably
ignorant of the objects of this labour organisation. The
fact should have been clearly apparent to you that, in the
'ery inception of these leagues, we distinctly refused to
know or entertain any party views, which had operated
'n the past to divide and render futile the efforts of the
Workers of this community iu the direction of legislative
reforms. The unwarrantable assumption you make iu
stating, in effect, that this labour organisation is literally
‘bossed” by Single-tax potentates, who flit about from
branch to branch, carrying a floating majority with
them, is such a manifest perversion that it needs little
’ n °re than a bare denial, and to draw your attention to
°ur platform and to express the desire, that as a brain-
worker you will fall into line with your more-lowly
co-workers, and then you would find your mind dis-
■llusioniaed-of the error you have given expression and
Publicity to. For yoft would then be aware of the fact
that most of our branch leagues have stringent hy-laws
m actual operation, which render it practically impossible
for any members who may favour any distinct outside
party to act in the manner you have asserted. In closing
I would point out that this attempt, on your part, to
unrig discredit mid disrepute upon this young, hut
vigorous organisation is, unwittingly perhaps, designed
to show to the workers, to the true democracy of tins
colony, that you have become alien to the democratic
instincts which have actuated you in the past, and the
blow you have blindly aimed at the workers’-party win

Secretary, O.C.L.E.L. of N.S.W.
The Bulletin has several things to mention in
regard to this excited epistle. (1.) It knows
exactly what are, or should be, the “objects
of this labour organisation.” As it advocated
these objects, and a lot more, for ton years
before the organisation Started, and is still
advocating them, and will advocate them as
long as it lives, it ought to know them. (2.)
This paper didn’t say that the said organisation
“ entertained any party views it said that
the Single-taxers entertained certain views, and
wanted to use the Labour Party to carry them
out, which is perfectly true —vide the recent
declarations of Single-tax Cotton, M.L.A. (3.)
It didn’t want the organisation to entertain
party views; that was why itobjected to it voting
the Foreign-trade ticket in consideration of an
alleged One- man- one- vote Bill, and then
throwing out Parkes (whom The Bulletin
has consistently opposed) and throwing away
the price of all its exertions. (4.) The
Bulletin didn’t say that the Labour Electoral
League is “bossed” by Single-taxers ; it
simply said that the latter individuals wanted
to boss it ; if Mr. Maston wanted to be a
Chinese gentleman, that doesn’t necessarily
imply that h.e is one. Therefore (5) the
League’s “bare denial” amounts to absolutely
nothing ; if the League sent along its denial in a
matter that solely concerned its own views, wo
would accept it cheerfully, but when one set of
men “denies” that another set of men enter-
tain certain unspoken aspirations, they are
denying more than they can be responsible for.
(6.) The League's “ stringent bye-laws ” have
nothing to do with the case ; 27,000 bye-laws
wouldn’t hinder the Single-tax people trying to
boss the League, and that is all The Bulletin
asserted. (7.) The Bulletin denies the
alleged “attempt” to “bring discredit and
disrepute ” on the Labour Electoral League;
its remarks were directed against the Single-
Tax-cnm-Foreign-Trade crowd, and unless Mr.
Maston is himself one of that crowd, and is
using the Labour Party as a tool, he has no
more cause to get in an ignorant frenzy over it
than has the Geographical Society, or the
Dorcas meeting, or the Cat-show committee.
(8.) The Bulletin ends by flatly contradicting
every other assertion in Mr. Maston’s letter
except the date and the signature, and by
remarking that it is perfectly indifferent to
both applause and denunciation —especially to
denunciation of the cant-democratic order, such
as is contained in the concluding paragraph of
Mr. Maston’s letter. Such figures of speech
as “ a blow rebounding .with increased force,”
are outrages on the laws of gravitation.
The Old Kiama Gliost.
The proposed establishment of a Protectionist-
policy in N.S.W. has struck terror and dismay
into the bloodless hearts of the Freetraders ;
and, as a last resource, the recognised leaders
of that faction are attempting to set the com-
munity by the ears. Then forlorn hope is
evidently to rekindle the fires of sectarianism,
and, with the aid of the Yellow Pup and the
plural vote, to block the march of Protection.
As the Evening News reserves its anti-
democratic views for its “society” paper, so
the Herald prints its genuine political opinions
in its evening offshoot, the Echo. The Herald
hasn’t the moral courage to raise the cry of
“ the Priest in Politics” in its own columns ;
but its proprietary hasn’t the slightest com-
punction in playing upon the religious pre-
judices of the “unco guid ” through the
medium of the Echo, as witness the following
extracts from that paper :
Brazil finds that the meddling of clericals in State
matters is intolerable and returns his passports to the
papal Nuncio. The Priest in politics again ! Wednesday.
The Jesuit in politics, and how he works, the labouring
classes in New South Wales will see when their delegates
record their votes on Mr. Reid's motion \ —Thursday.

of Federation.” It is now the holy cause of Protection
which has the blessings of the Church and Mr. Barton's
special attention. — Friday.

To allay the financial panic existent in Mel-
bourne, a special measure was rushed through
both Houses of the Victorian Parliament, just
when public alarm was at its acutest stage.
The Bill was framed to prevent a Judge from
ordering the compulsory liquidation of any
company that has undertaken voluntary
liquidation except at the request of the
creditors—one-third both as to number and
value. This is a most arguable course to take, and
the action was only taken because Capital teas
harassed. Now, had Labour been suffering from
any equally acute strain, let it be asked if any
Parliament would have strained its loins to
rush through any remedial Bill in one day ?
No, nor in one week, nor one month, nor one
year. Masses may choke, starve, be trampled
underfoot, and “ it is all right.” “ Times will
right themselves,” &c., &c. But let Capital get
a sore toe, or a whitlow on its little finger, and
Creation reverberates its bowlings, and—
Parliament cures the ill in one act.
The “run ” on the various Melbourne building
societies perhaps dates from the day when the
Age sent a sympathetic interviewer to wait
upon Matthias Larkin. Depositors were then
seized by a fear that embezzlement would come
to be regarded as a short cut to the Temple of
Fame. A flood of “ Premier Association case ”
recollections rushed into their harassed minds,
and. taking one thing with another, they
decided to withdraw their cash before any more
secretaries and directors had attempted to win
martyrs’ crowns. The Age, it is satisfactory to
find, has now thrown Matthias over as a bad
job, and prints painful particulars of his moral
“trip” in comparatively cold blood. But
nobody can doubt that the effect of “ our special
reporter’s ” interview, coming after the anti-
Templeton crusade of a year ago, was to set
folks wondering whether frauds on building-
societies had been struck off the list of legal
offences. And although they still had every
confidence in the integrity, &c., of everybody,
there was no getting away from the fact that a
secretary who can get five years' when he goes
wrong is more trustworthy than the gentleman
who can loot the treasury with impunity.
Wherefore depositors rushed in for their hard-
earned savings and payment was promptly—
Suspended.
One of the stock arguments against the See
tariff—which, as it can hardly be called a
scientific Protectionist one, The Bulletin
does not uphold in every item —is that, while
the New South Wales Treasurer only
hopes to get £75,000 from the imposts on what
lie styles “ luxuries,” he estimates receiving
five or six times as much from “ the taxes
which the poor people must pay.” Even
Parkes thought this sort of humbug good
enough for Kiama. Give these people the
benefit of their premises and follow the facts:—-
Luxuries produce £75,000; the other taxes,
say, even as much as £500,000, which is reck-
lessly overdoing it. Who pay the £75,000 ? The
well-to-do. Who pay the half-million ? The
poor and the w'ell-to-do. The well-to-do may
be set down at one-twentieth of the population,
and the poor at nineteen-twentieths. Now, the
whole twenty-twentieths find the £500,000 —
say, roughly, a million souls at 10s. per head.
But only olie-twentieth pay the £75,000, or
50,000 souls at 80s. per head. These' 50,000
have already paid 10s. per head with the rest ;
so their whack to the revenue is £2 per head.
The figures, on the basis taken, would be
950,000 poor at 10 s. per head .. £475,000
50,000 well-to-do at £2 per head 100,000
Total £575,000
Thus, although the aggregate poor total up
more than the aggregate rich, the individual
poor man only pays one-fourth the taxes of the
individual rich.
In the recent Colston murder-case at Mel-
bourne, counsel for the defence took up a per-
fectly rational position, viz. : the existence of
brain-disease, which all the medical witnesses
admitted, though differing in detail, and the
impulse which marked that disease was
acknowledged by all. But the Judge con-
tinually put forward the grotesquely illogical
right and wrong theory—he told the jury that
the homicide himself said he knew when he
killed his victims that he was doing wrong, and
he remarked that there could be no better
proof of a man’s condition than his own state-
ment. In another Victorian case the other day
there occurred a peculiar incident bearing
strongly on the arguments in the Colston case.
Mrs. Scarsbrook, of Richmond, who poisoned
herself and three children (one or more of the
latter fatally) in a fit of puerperal madness,
said she knew at the time she was doing wrong,
and meant to do it, but she could not help it—
she “had to do it.” This knowledge of hers
was brought out prominently at her trial for
murder and much dwelt upon ; counsel for the
defence logically asked the jury to find the
woman sane and guilty or not guilty at all.
But the Judge— Higinbotham, C.J.—saw the
position, and told the jury that it was for them
to say whether, when she made the statement —
which she repeated to several persons, including
the doctors—she knew what she was saying ?
Thus do Judges save themselves from the
logical outcome of their present irrational
theory that a prisoner is necessarily morally
and legally responsible for his or her actions
whenever he or she knows the difference between
right and wrong.
We condense from a daily newspaper report; —
John Maskall, a decent-looking young fellow against
whom the police know nothing, was staying at a Sydney
hotel, and one morning left his room partially nude to go
to the bath. While in the passage leading bath-wards he
heard on the stairs what he took to be feminine footsteps
and ran into the first room—which happened to bethatof
the landlord. As fate would have it, the footsteps were
those of the landlady, and when Maskall found her
entering the room he slipped under the bed to await her
departure. She saw his feet and screamed, whereupon
he arose and caught hold of the lady’s wrist lightly, tried
to explain, implored her not to make such a noise, and
putting her aside hastened back to his room. The police
were sent for, Maskall was given in charge for being
illegally in the room—but the police-magistrate (after
ascertaining that accused had a towel with him) kindly
altered the charge to “ assault!” and allotted prisoner
three months hard labour.
Now, the question as to the towel clearly
showed that doubt existed in the magistrate’s
mind. May nob a great injustice have been
here done to a modest young man ? The Bul-
letin urges upon the Minister of Justice that
any jury would have given Maskall the benefit
of the doubt.
Dear Bulletin, —After all, the Federal Council
(discarded but still existent) ottered the most
natural mode of growth to the Federal idea.
Australian Federation may be likened to an
economic marriage of States, and no marriage
can hope to result in lasting happiness that is
suddenly forced upon hitherto strangers with
mutual differentiations and antipathies. The
rrf/ime of the Federal Council represented the
precautionary period of experimental courtship,
during which the high contracting parties to
the final ceremony acquire the necessary give-
and-take knowledge of each other’s idiosyn-
cracies. Affinities common to each formed the
first points of friendly contact, and with the
deepening intimacy of intercolonial statesmen
fresh tentacles of union were only a question of
time. The Federal Council promised an easy
process of development, a step-by-step ascent to
the summit of perfect union. The wounded pique
of the vain old matchmaker, Parkes, who wanted
to hastily marry the parties willy-nilly, was the
chief factor in retarding the expansion of the
Federal Council idea and in limiting the useful-
ness of that body. Because Parkes had not
been conspicuous in its first hooray, he em-
ployed all his political influence and cunning to
stunt its growth and to hinder its recognition
by the mother-colony. His subsequent vain-
glorious failure to model out a Federation from
the clay of his own feet carries with it his suffi-
cient punishment. Now that the Parkes
scheme is laid upon the shelf, the Federal
Council may be utilised with beneficial, prac-
tical results.—H. C.
In connection with the recent passing of about
£3OO worth of Bank of Adelaide forged £1 notes,
that institution advertised loftily that it would
feel obliged if the victimised holders would visit
the bank with their loathsome debentures, in
which case the institution would be so good
as to cancel those which were spurious and re-
initial those which might turn out good. The
dailies wherein this impudent manifesto occurs
cap the affair by observing that anyone holding
a bogus note had better be very careful and
comply with the bank’s request, otherwise he
or she may incur the liability of “uttering”
should the note get into circulation. Now,
what we have to say on this subject is short,
but unmistakeable. The class of people “had ”
in this paltry swindle consists mainly of small
tradesmen, and it is bad enough for these to
have to drop even 205., without having to leave
their businesses to favour a supercilious institu-
tion whose only fear is" lest some of its own
clerks should happen to cash forgeries. Divid-
ing the loss of £3OO among the bank’s share-
holders, the yearly dividend would be affected
to no more than a fractional extent, and, as it
must have been through the bank’s fault that
the forgeries were rendered achievable, it is
clear that, both as a matter of policy and equity,
the bank should stand the racket and give a
golden sovereign in exchange for the miserable
pieces of paper which hona fide citizens have
cashed. A remedy for preventing similar mis-
chances in future is obvious : Decline to accept
notes of any find from anyone, and then how
will the associated pawnbrokers feel? When
Ave get a State Bank it will probably be the
rule that the community at large shall suffer
the losses of those individuals who may happen
to be deceived by occasional forgeries, and it
will be the duty of those who pay the piper to
detect and punish the criminals, and not as it
is now, i.efi the duty of the victimised trades-
man. To this end let us quick march.
Sir William M'Gregor, the autocrat of the
missionary-haunted, snuffle-burdened, priest-
infested land of New Guinea, has issued anew
law, whereby any native guilty of adultery
shall be imprisoned for six months, and his
female accomplice for three. Presumably he has
done this at the instance of the white-chokered
crowd of wild preachers who' so horribly per-
vade the island—nobody but a preacher would
get down to such an act of tyranny. For, in
the first place, there is no particular or well-
defined marriage-law or custom in the country,
therefore the M'Gregor can’t tell with any
certainty what is immorality and what is
ordinary matrimony. Asa natural conse-
quence, when the naked Romeo gets married,
it is three chances to one that a huge, raw
Presbyterian dressed hi a little passing authority
will straightway put him and his wife
in gaol through sheer ignorance. Secondly,
there is no such law in Great Britain ;
the self-governing Englishman reserves to him-
self the right to commit adultery without
incurring any criminal penalty, and it is a mean,
low proceeding for him to pile up on a feeble
little nigger the burden of a code of morality
which he won’t carry himself. The Englishman
governs the New Guinea nigger, and preaches
and prays at him, and professes to be a superior
being generally, and then he demands that this
same despised heathen shall be better than he is
himself, or else go to gaol. It is a despicable
and wormy proceeding all round, and the cast-
iron saint who first conceived the idea—the
black-coated, black-hatted person with the holy
smile—should be turned out naked in a primeval
forest, if only to see how moral he could be
when he also was reduced to first principles.
The attention of the present New South Wales
Minister of Justice is directed to the case of
Thomas Suffield, convicted at last Maitland
Assizes of a criminal assault upon a woman
named Ann Chambers, a domestic in the em-
ploy of Mr. Thos. Croudace, J.P., and sen-
tenced to ten years’ penal servitude. The
evidence was extremely weak, and the discre-
pancies between the statements made by prosecu-
trix at the Police Court and at the sessions were
glaring. The accused, a young man of previously
irreproachable character, set up a strong alibi.
There was no circumstantial evidence of the
alleged deed found upon him, and his time was
so well accounted for that the verdict was irre-
concileable with common sense. The girl first
imputed her injuries to a ferocious bull of Mr.-
Croudace’s, which subsequently hurt another
woman, who received compensation, the bull
then being destroyed. The evidence was as
weak as the judicial summing-up was unfair.
The very advantages which nature had bestowed
upon Suffield were turned to his prejudice
and ruin by the Judge, who suggested the
case of Colonel Valentine Baker to the minds
of the jury in proof that good looks, education,
and intelligence did not necessarily keep any
man from crime.

8-16 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Dec. 9,1891,
My Dear. Moorauinda,—
The day was hot and moist and windy when the ’Ome
cricketers first appeared at Sydney Association Ground.
Dr. Grace stood out as the largest, baggiest hero of the
troupe, and as he plodded heavily along with his hairy
arms folded across his chest, he made me think vaguely
of the “belligerent hippopotami” Mr. Stanley talks
about. Individualised, the visiting team seemed to con-
sist, as usual, of the athletic young man with no percep-
tible forehead—and the game he played was doubtless
heavenly' cricket full of thrill and craft and genius, but,
after all, when the brightly r -clad performers had moved
one to momentary' enthusiasm as they' strode to the wicket,
little remained but stifled yawning fits to keep the lay'
observer going.
As play did not begin until three-quarters of an hour
late, owing to a dispute over the umpiring, dresses and
people were easily sorted out. Among the frocked con-
tingent Mrs. (Dr.) Grace wore a dark blue dress, figured
in white, small black bonnet; Mrs. Victor Cohen, grey
frieze with invisible rainbow check of blue and pink,
trimmed with revers of garnet silk, black bonnet. White
muslin, with a little red dot, trimmed with red muslin
embroidery, was nice until you got to her hat, which
appeared to have been boiled ; a neat pale-grey frock was
nicely contrasted with a scarlet gamp, and the pink roses
in the hat were quite in taste according to latest styles,
but the artistic eye was pained. The most artful
combination was a terra-cotta cloth topped by a fawn
straw hat crowded with poppies.
Numbers of Australians will regret the death (in
America, from Bright’s disease) of Mr. G. W. Griffin, U.S.
Consul at Sydney for many years, though the cabled
news was no shock to his near friends, as the Counsel
avowedly' went home to the Stales in. order to die in his
native land. It must be a gruesome, dreary' business—
ploughing drearily over the desolate seas which seem to
lap hungrily for your bones as the ship races against
death to port, and Consul Griffin hated what was grue-
some or dreary. The brightest recollection I have of him
was at a ball, sailing up the room with a lady on cither
arm—which was not strange, only one lady looked as
pleased and flattered as the other, which was marvellous.
Any theoretical ladies’-man who underrates this little
tribute to the social qualities of Gilderoy Wells Griffin
will greatly appreciate it if he trots out two ladies before
passing his opinion.
It is a dreadful task to have to write in an outwardly
calm manner that Australia is going to lose Admiral Pre-
cedence Scott some time next year. He will be in Heaven
—promotion to the rank of Vice-Admiral is Heaven to a
man of his order—on or about September, ’92, and it is
said that Admiral Stephenson is to rush the Orlando
about from Cup to Cup in his stead. Admiral Stephenson
"has been unemployed since his promotion, 14 months
ago, and though this appointment would involve the
passing over of several seniors, Admiral Stephenson is an
excellent officer, and is considered to have strong claims,
not the least of which is his connection with the Prince of
Wales’ household as equerry.” Mr. S. seems to be quite a
shocking example of official favouritism. As a con-
spicuous unemployed, he is sent hurtling over the grey
heads of his snubbed seniors just because he has daw'dled
about Wales’ premises, keeping the flies off a fat sinecure
and perhaps “ obliged his royal master on pressing occa-
sions.” One could almost grow indignant at the way of
the “ appointment,” only it is such a matter of indiffer-
ence who occupies the patchy mansion at Kirribilli Point,
and Mr. Stephenson won’t want for thorns on his roses.
Some people would rather be subordinate all their lives
than have (in the absence of a Government grant for eau-
de-cologne) to live next door to a hide-warehouse.
Talking about Admirals, what does an Admiral do?
Act the frowning Providence to so many jack-tars and
whisk his gold-braided coat-tails round the marble halls
of the retired pork-butcher in the stuccoed suburb. The
rest of the time goes in squabbling over little side-winds
of ceremony and seeing that the flag gets dipped the
proper number of times when he appears any where sud-
denly on the brink of the harbour, and that’s nearly the
sum total of his trying duties. It must be as easy as
breathing to be an Admiral once you’re in. You needn’t
even be a good sailor, for most of the Admirals we become
familiar with in Australia travel per Pullman car when-
ever they have the chance, while the vessel creeps round
the coast and the faithful newspapers report that the
Admiral passed Barrenjoey at 4 p m.
Owing to a vertical drizzle at starting time I missed the
Sydney articled-clerks’ mock-trial, “Boyant v. Merri-
man,” which eventuated at the stepping-heavenward-like
Y.M.C.A. Hall, on 3d Inst., but 1 don’t think I dodged
much fun. The mock-case (boiled down) before the court
«as that the two yachts collided—one went down, and
the man and his best girl, who got ducked, went around
in a damp, fishy state howling for damages. I really don’t
see how the audience could get much thrill over a dreary
worry of that sort, do you ? Perhaps the articled sharks
made the thing snappy and full of “go”—it must have
been as laborious as wheeling a barrow of sand uphill on
a holiday if they did, and it seems a pity they didn’t
sharpen their wits to get a more lively subject. Are
they too bowdlerised to put up a breach-of-promise action
with its myriad larky possibilities? The last theatrical
mock-trial held in Sydney was a B. of P. suit, and great
was the hilarity thereof. George Anson was the blighted
damsel, and you could hear the “court ” laughing on the
other side of the street.
Going to see the Juvenile operatists at the “Criterion,"
In a day and generation when you receive a daily invita-
tion to go and watch the capers of the public-school
Infant gambolling on a platform to raise money to buy
itself a prize at Xmas—the “novelty” doesn’t seem such
a blessed change as it did in the years when children were
publicly seen and not heard. Children’s opera is soothing
in one way. It is lovely to see “Three Little Maids” (it
was “Mikado,” by the way) who really looked as if they
had just come from school. Also, the school-girl chorus,
Each a little bit afraid is, wondering what the world can
be, is not at all aggravating when sung by a fresh-faced
crowd, of small girls, instead of by a horde of ogling,
paint-worn hags, each of whom looks as if she knew
enough to score points off a divorce-lawyer. The“pug-
wuggies ” are dressed correctly, but who on earth paints
their little faces ? Somebody who places them in line
and throws their blushes at them with a trowel, judging
by results. Some of the infants could sing if their voices
weren’t overworked, but their acting bears the imprint of
the stage-manager, and you can almost see it written on
t.ie margin of their part: “ Here you wink the other eye.”
And who would have it otherwise ? The girl of twelve,
who played Yum-Yum to encore business, would make
every woman in the house long to take her home and
spank her as a stern moral duty.

Monday afternoon—the last day of Grace—the attend-
ance totalling 10,000. There w'ere bevies of Society
butterflies (they patronise everything English) in Sunday
clothes, all jumping and shrieking like ’Arriet out for a
oliday, as the flannelled figures in the open played to the
gallery. The Y.R.-box in the middle of the Grand-Stand
was well filled and gaped at all the time. The central
cynosure was the very latest “ colonial-experiencer”
Lady Galloway—a plump and amiable aristocrat with a
profile like a China doll, who made her Australian dehiit
in a cream gown with a frisky-looking black hat trimmed
in a profuse way with black feathers, beads and pink
roses. She and Lady Innes (looking charming in a black
gown with bead edgings, neck scarf of heliotrope chiffon
and tiny black bonnet with wreath of purple heath) took
care of the two Jersey children in cream frocks and white
hats and feathers.
Under the cegis of the senior member for somewhere I
got a cosy seat behind what Max O’Rell calls the “spoken
to”atN.S,W. Legislative Assembly the night when the

jackets and shirts, with big solitaires in the wristbands,
who didn’t listen over-intently to the talk, but seemed to
imagine that you achieved Parliamentary savoir faire by
steeping yourself for ever so long in the hot atmosphere
of the Assembly, as in a sort of Turkish bath of Legisla-
tive procedure. Viewing the shapers of N.S.W. political
destiny in their native lair, it becomes evident that Sir
Henry Parkes is the only member who effectively
“dresses” the part and successfully imparts the pic-
turesque into public life. By the way, I don’t think I
ever yet heard a woman say a word against Sir Henry.
“ Why?” As if you didn’t know !
A friend of mine, a politician, tells me that Sir Henry’s
extreme nastiness in the recent South Coast speech, of the
vulgarity of which everyone is talking, was perhaps
partially owing to the mixed character of the brands of
champagne submitted for the inspection of the company
during the afternoon. Another reason was that while
the “Hairy Bismarck”—is not that what the Albury
German called him?—was telling the audience in some-
what affected and almost lachrymose tones what ’e did
hin “ fifty-height ” a man who wanted medical assistance
for his wife entered the packed hall, and, unable to make
his way to the front, called out for “ Doctor Breretonl ”
Sir Henry took the interjector for a Dibbsite and an
enemy, opened upon him the floodgates of abuse, and
called him a “ hidjit ” and a “ hinterrupting brute.”
Almost all professional Sydney, and a lot of mere
private individuals, drifted to the sad sea-shore on 13th
instant, to bang hollow palms at Mr. Alfred Wyburd on
the occasion of the opening of the newly-rebuilt Bondi
Aquarium. After the concert, the shaggy donkey held a
levee on the lawn ; the seals and the people bumping over
the “Switchback” shrieked in concert, and the crowd
surged everywhere.
Landlords should hesitate before letting a house to a
medical man, unless he shows signs of taking root for
ever, because victims who inhabit directly after a doctor
live to bitterly repent of their rashness ! A well-known
Sydney “dashing militaire,” who moved in after a
registrar-and-medico in one, has had dismal proof of the
horrors of the situation, and his real woes may act as
warnings to the house-father who ventures to precipitate
his goods and chattels under the sinister red-lamp
shadowing the bold brass plate. For instance, at
one a.m., as he sank into his beauty-sleep, the friends of
the last horrible suicide would hang on to his bell-handle
and implore him to come down and inspect the gashes;
and their frantic appeals for aid would be interrupted by
the low meanings of the man who had brought round a
compound-fracture in a cab and wanted it examined im-
mediately, and when the badgered house-holder had dis-
tinctly rectified the mistake from an upper-window, and
gone back to bed, a happy father would come streaking
round the corner and bang him up in his capacity as
registrar, and inform the listening street, betweep tattoos,
that the nurse said it weighed nine pounds. Then in the
daytime his drawing-room was mobbed by giggling
servant-girls and heavily-breathing swains who wanted to
“git married” for two-and-six, and this interesting
crowd, going out, would be met by the results of a shriek-
ing tram-accident coming in, and life had no charm for
the registrar-doctor’s successor until he hung out a brass-
plate with his name and titles on it in bold letters. I
could tell you a much better story about the consequences
of taking a house without carefully enquiring as to who
had preceded you, but on second reflection I must decline
to commit it to paper.

round. It didn’t increase them when it was
making the colossal profits which turned the train-
ring into millionaires. The ring has eaten up
all the profits, and now the man who works plays
the part of the whipped eat.

You don’t often see Mr. Pilcher, Q.C., in
the Criminal Court, but he was there last week to
defend T. M. Halveen, erstwhile well-known in
N.S.W. political circles, on a charge of malicious
wounding. The case arose out of the fact that
during a quarrel a man’s eye happened to be
injured by Dalveen’s umbrella. Defendant was
acquitted, and there appears very little doubt
that the occurrence was purely accidental.
By whose authority do the Sydney dog-
catchers fish for the wretched animals with large
fish-hooks baited with a hunk of meat ? This
horrible cruelty is perpetrated every day in a city
where a market-woman who ties two hens to-
gether by the legs is pounced upon and fined, and
the man who judiciously wallops a jibbing
horse is looked upon as a monster for whom the
gibbet would be too tender a punishment.
This was the telegram Swogley got :
“Come and see me ; I’m dying.” He took a cab
post haste, and found Swigley awaiting to welcome
him on the doorstep. “Thought you were dying?”
“So I am. Dying—for a drink. And you’re just
the boy to save my life.” Next day, when
Swogley got a similar telegram, he merely wired
a reply—“ Die and be d——This time it was
his rich uncle who was dying, and who did die
after scratching his nephew out of his will.
One of those harpies who run libellous
trade-circulars, recording bills of sale &c. the other
day summoned a man’s widow and executrix for
the “subscription to the society.” He produced
an order purporting to be signed by the lately
deceased party, and never blenched or committed
suicide when it was unmistakably proved that the
old man never wrote a single letter of the alphabet
in his life, for the simple reason that he did not
know how.
Frank Cotton, M.L.A., declares that he
will never consent to vote for Protection, no
matter what the Labour caucus may decide. Of
what earthly use, then, is a Labour caucus, if
Cotton (or anyone else) can attend on the heads-I
(single-taxer)-win-tails-you-lose principle? Cot-
ton’s statement, and the decision of the Newtown
Labour League (presided over by his pal, Single-
tax Johnston), practically declaring that Cotton
is to be at liberty to vote as he pleases on fiscal
questions, prove that the N.S.W. Labour Elec-
toral League is being used as a tool by the Single-
taxers.
Albert Victor, it is cabled, is to marry
Princess Victoria of Teck. As usual, it is the
biggest fool who weds the prettiest girl. The
Times hopes that ere she dies Queen Victoria will
be able to place her hand on the head of a child
who “will wear the Crown as third in order of
succession to herself.” Queen Victoria should be
happy then. She will see her three possible suc-
cessors left to fight the advancing Republic—a fat,
discredited sensualist; his son, a gawky imbecile
who doesn’t know enough to come in out of the
rain ; and the heir thereof, the attenuated result
of the in-breeding of—the Guelphs, a term which,
in itself, exhausts the vocabulary of invective.
Major Rae, the circus midget, is greater
than most husbands, for he is lord and master
over his dutiful spouse. On a recent Sunday,
while at the Zoo, they were given some porcupine-
quills and emu feathers. On arriving home the
little woman began to pick her teeth with one of
the quills. “ Don’t do that, Jennie !” commanded
the Majah. Jennie paid no heed, so the irate
bantam seized the quill and wrenched it away
by brute force. The affectionate little woman
retaliated by cuddling up to her lord, tickling his
face with the emu feathers, and murmuring, “I
will teaze you for that, Major, indeed I will.”
When Dom Pedro, the ex-Emperor of
Brazil (he died the other day), visited Europe
some years ago, he did not change his
linen during periods of from a week to
ten clays, concealing the manifestations of this
neglect by means of a most extraordinary double-
breasted waistcoat with numerous buttons. In
proportion as the week advanced and the appear-
ance of his shirt-front became less immaculate, be
would increase the number of buttons used, until
on the sixth and seventh days the waistcoat was
buttoned right up close to the collar, leaving,
perhaps fortunately, no portion of the shirt-front
visible. The Emperor’s waistcoat, indeed, con-
stituted a species of calendar, enabling one to see
the days of the week.;— Argonaut.
Miss Valerie Wiedemann, the heroine of
the famous Walpole seduction and breach of
promise case, is now one of the leading cranks of
London. She is a faded, bilious-looking, young
woman with watery eyes, and a haggard,
monomaniacal expression, who drifts about the
city, selling concert-tickets to raise the wind for
more litigation. She button-holes strange men in
the streets, and backs them against walls, and
tells them the story of how Walpole ought to have
married her but didn’t, and when she is seen
coming along with her tale of woe and her tickets,
she can clear a building faster than an alarm of
fire. As for Walpole himself he is bankrupt
through law costs and damages, and has bolted to
the continent almost without ts. And here
endeth a big romance.

Stanley is still in Sydney, and still
telling- his dry and heartrending narrative of that
mystio continent, where unpronounceable names
chase eacli other over the sands, and shattered
consonants trample wildly upon crippled vowek
as they tear up and down the pages of the ereit
explorer s manuscript. The lecturer got down to
his saddest and dreariest level when he told th«
tale of how he shoved his uncalled-for personalitv
upon Livingstone. He had apparently gone over
this lecture with care, and torn up every item of
interest by the roots, and he fired off thedry bones
and remnants of the story with a dreary, mono
tonous sound like a brass image groaning in a nit
1 here is much expression of a certain kind in
Stanley s voice, but it isn’t suited to his present
surroundings. It ought to be heard in a ruined
and desolate city, under a glaring sun and sur
rounded by glaring yellow sand—a waterless ruin
with snakes and lizards in it all crawling
wretched y oyer the burning rocks. Then there
ought to be skeletons here and there, and a dessi
cated corpse in a tattered uniform and a broken
hat leaning up against the ramparts, and looking
with a dead eye through a warped and battered
telescope in search of the assistance that never
comes, and listening with a dead ear to the silence
of the buried ages. There should be haggard
ghosts floating around on fleshless feet, and sliZ
things made up of hair, and claws, and innumer
able legs, and bodies consisting principally 0 f
horrid squash and slime, should shuffle gloomilv
around, and shake their black and fire-blasted
front legs at each other in malevolent frenzy. Anri
in the midst of these cheerless scenes the great
explorer should sit on a dictionary and tell his
dismal tale in that voice which sounds so much
like the endless drip of falling water on top of a
hanged man’s skull. Then he would be eminently
suited to his surroundings, and his surrounding
wou d be eminently suited to him, and there
would be a great calm.
* * *
But when Stanley got to the end of his
story concerning how he found Livingstone and
started to tell how he found Emin instead he
woke up to unexpected energy. His delivery was
as monotonous as before, and the matter of his
discourse was full of the same solemn wretched-
ness which always pervades it, but there was an
undercurrent of rage and fathomless malevolence
winch kept the audience awake. From first to
last he did not utter a word concerning the
rescued ” Pasha which did not contain either
hatred, reproach, or contempt. The adventurous
scientist, who went almost alone into the wilder-
ness, and there built up a great kmgdom out of
unspeakable savagery, anarchy, and all manner
of hopeless elements—-who held his own against
all-comers for years and years without outside
assistance, and whose history is one of the most
astonishing features in the tale of modern Africa
-according to Stanley this individual was a fool
of the meanest order, a person only one remove
from an idiot, whose real place in life was that of
hanger-on to some society of ancient females,
ihe doings of Emin Pasha give the lie
direct to every word of this story, and
Stanley stands out conspicuously throughout
the narrative as a frantic ivory-hunter who feels
bad at not getting the plunder. The really inter-
esting features of the story are omitted, as usual.
1 he abandoned rear-guard, left behind under the
charge of a madman in a swamp, is practically
dropped out of sight. So is the language which
Stanley used to the wretched survivors when to
save their lives, they threw away the great man’s
hair-dye and personal luxuries. The alleged long
track of corpses which marked the road through
the great forest is not mentioned to any great
extent; and the explorer merely barks forth the
statement that on emerging from the wooded
region he crossed a great grassy plain, and camped
in a place 2000 feet above the level of the sea, and
the ghastly slaughter which is said to have taken
place on that same plain isn’t even alluded to.
After everyone of these statements the speaker
gets back to curse Emin afresh, and then he flies
off into more dreary facts, and rushes back to
curse Emin some more. And all through he is
obviously suffering under a disadvantage, for his
vocabulary is short, and his curses are far from
copious, and he suggests a man with a bad stutter
frantically shaking a lump of fat at Providence
because the remark that he wants to make has got
wrecked in his back teeth. Therefore, The Bul-
letin urges him to leap on his hat-or on Smythe’s
hat—at the more exciting parts of the discourse,
so as to relieve his feelings and to fill the gap
when words fail him. For if he doesn’t leap on
something, this paper is of opinion that he may
burst, and leave the Much-Travelled one without
any brigand.

“The Grasshopper” is rubbishy, but,
nevertheless, it has been so ably handled during
past productions in Sydney that, up till now, it
enjoyed a reputation which Jennie Lee’s staging of
it at Sydney Garrick is likely somewhat to impair.
Hot onlyAs it indifferently cast, but a thin and

plippit; the atrocious Hielan’of Jimmy Hasker

gathered pile of Caledonian chestnuts, and the
first appearance of Frank Hawthorn in his old
man’s theatre. Jennie Lee, as wonderful in her
way as Nellie Farren, is bright and lively as ever,
jifow that “Jo ” has almost outworn a popularity
0 f 20 years’ standing, the little woman might pro-
fitably devote her attention to burlesque. “ Grass-
hopper ” is well staged; the backcloth, a river
scene, of the second act is very beautiful. The
Oogill Bros, come along with their Comedy and
Burlesque Co. on Saturday night.

Bondi Aquarium was officially opened last
week with a great flourish of trumpeters and
other inhabitants of the deep. Everyone of con-
sequence, and heaps of unconsequential people
were present, some armed with tickets of invita-
tion, others armed with equally persuasive bobs.
Refreshments of all kinds were served up in the
Club-house to the invitees, and after a big pro-
gramme had been gone through in the theatre,
the fish-tanks were inspected. The tanks are full
of every kind of fish known to iohthyophagi, and
a great many sorts unknown as edibles, but fair
to look on. Bondi, now better than new, is bound
to boom along while the hot weather lasts.

Rink shortly as a Vienna Summer Garden with
a series of bright and breezy entertainments.
Audiences will sit round little tables where coffee,

Simousen, Mrs. Tudor, Alice Austin, the Blair
Sisters and Bovis Bros. Fred. B. Norton will
look after the business arrangements.

Shakespeare has made another attempt to assert
himself at Melbourne Royal, but circumstances
were much against him, and the immortal bard’s
latest lease of life ran out on Saturday night. A
season of four consecutive nights was quite suffi-
cient for Divine William this time. No slim,
experienced actor, possessed of average intelli-
gence and laudable ambition can manage to,fail
egregiously as Hamlet, but Bentley gets very near
to that achievement. He dresses and poses like a
dandy, yet talks, at most times, like a hard,
matter-of-fact young Scotchman, either because he
°f that persuasion, or as the result of his
having played so much with Scotch companies
before Scotch audiences, and frequently in a Hee-
land character. Bentley’s unnustakeable accent
comes as a shook to anyone who remembers his
attractive Lyceum performance of Clarence, in
“Richard the Third,” years ago. In other
respects, too, he has gone from good to worse.
Desperately anxious to avoid speaking his
soliloquies after the manner of other Hamlets he
frequently allows the meaning of the lines to get
lost, and is incomprehensible in his originality.
There are no convincing signs of weakness and
melancholy about this business-like young man,
notwithstanding an occasional snivel and a
feminine wipe of his eye with a handkerchief, nor
does he suggest the awestruck condition of a
loving son who prowls out at midnight to converse
with t’ould man’s spectre. His vigorous dis-
courtesy and petulance, effective in the interviews
with Rosencrantz and Guildenstern, are elsewhere
out of place—in short, Bentley’s performance of
Hamlet seems to suffer by comparison witli
many others that can be called to mind. A
Miss Laura Hansen, with a weak but sym-
pathetic voice and manner, made a negative
success as Ophelia. James Cathcart was, of
course, a very satisfactory Polonius, and Clifton
Alderson, as the Ghost, managed to get over the
ground in a more spectral manner than might
have been expected. The support in general was
of an exasperating nature, however, and the first
gravedigger deserves a word of reprobation for not
digging a larger hole and burying the whole crowd,
himself included. On Saturday night Mr. Bentley
appeared as Matthias, in “ The Bells,” but a tele-
gram on this subject was not urgently called for.
