“ Hue and Cry ” struggles along at Mel"
bourne Opera House, and will probably hold the
boards until Saturday, anyhow. The softness of
this Darrellian yarn is not immediately apparent)
so the audience feel kindly disposed towards
George before he pops up in high life and becomes
wearisome. Charles Holloway, in the part of an
unsober ruin, is a general favourite ; also, an im-
possibly comic Chinaman attracts attention, as a
member of that ancient race which is destined to
conquer Australia ere another 4000 years have
passed away. A Melbourne journalist has just
called attention to this coming calamity, and it
behoves every native of the soil to start the
defence of his native land by killing the Chinese
invaders of his back-yard without delay.
f $
The revival of “Marjorie,” at Melbourne
Princess, doesn’t make the original failure, or
comparative failure, of this handsome opera a
matter for surprise. It is now a little better than
the “Marjorie” of a year ago, inasmuch as a
weak third act has been pruned of some dialogue
and an interpolated pas-de-seul by Flora Graupner
is carefully left out, but the main fault of the
show is beyond the reach of any stage manager.
Some of the characters were conceived in a
serious, romantic spirit by one librettist, whilst
his partner was seemingly engaged in merging the
sublime into the ridiculous. The effect is inhar-
monious and irritating to a degree—as a work
of art, “ Marjorie ” can only be compared
with a piece of streaky bacon. Its serious
tableaux at the end of the first and second acts
are indeed very fine and mediaeval, but they
merely serve' to bring the story as a whole into
contempt, magnifying the absurdity of a chaotic
conclusion. Consistently good music might have
excused the libretto to some extent, but as most of
the melody is thin and tawdry, and the incidental
ballads have no immediate bearing on the subject,
the composer counts for very little more than in
an opera like “ The Old Guard.” Which reminds
us that a spicy ballet is badly wanted in this in-
stance. Girls didn’t kick high under the Feudal
System, but anachronisms can be freely pardoned
where leers are concerned, therefore a bevy of
Saxon maidens can-can ing around the tyrant
Norman earl would wake things up consider-
ably. Charles Ryley is a pleasant, picturesque
nobleman in the armour as of yore, whilst
Flora Graupner, Elsie Cameron, Elton, Bracy,
and Howard Vernon also do their duty again by
their former characters. The name part, well
sung by Florence Saville last time, introduced
Miss Marie Elster to general notice, on Saturday.
This lady, who has already faced audiences in
concert performances, was as shy as Miss Amelia
Martin on her first appearance in comic opera,
and after she had “ come out ” there seemed to
be much danger of her tottering in again and
seeking refuge behind the scenery. Miss Marie can
take heart, however. She has a pretty, unsophis-
ticated smile and manner, and her voice only
needs multiplying by two in order to make a
theatre ring again. It is a sweet and, doubtless,
an effective organ when she pulls out all the stops.
“Marjorie,” being almost a novelty, should last
over next week, by which time th •. . ia donna’s
prospects will be made clearer.
m* * *
Grundy’s clover bub painfully artificial
comedy, “ The Silver Shield,” was presented to a
crowded Bijou on Saturday. The story, already
familiar to Sydney, deals with hearts blighted on
unsubstantial evidence, and the weakness of
jealous wives for taking to the stage at enormous
salaries. The moral seems to be that a lady who
is half an idiot in domestic life can rush away and
play leading characters in drama, which is rather
rough on the drama. Matrimonial misunder-
standings arise as easily as smoke from a
chimney in Grundy’s play, and a fond matron
no sooner picks up a suspicious - looking
letter, addressed to nobody, than she puts
on her “things” and wanders off into the
darkness, after bidding a tearful farewell to the
family piano, and the coal-scuttle, and all the
other bits of furniture she has learned to love so
well. Previously she has purposely dropped her
wedding-ring upon the dear old carpet, as a token
that she has been married for the last time. Then
another lone, lost wife finds the ring, and
remembering that she also used to chuck her
golden circlet about when she was jealous, grasps
the horror of the situation just in time to be too
late. Luckily circumstances are ridiculously
favorable to an all-round explanation, and the
parted couples become reunited. Their belief in
the advantages of matrimony is somewhat pathetic.
The Brough and Boucicault company, who do
nothing badly, are moderately successful in “ The
Silver Shield.” It runs for six nights only, and
on Saturday there will be a first Bijou perform-
ance of “Led Astray” by the whole strength of
the B. and B. forces.
* * *
At the Alexandra they are still supping on
the reckless atrocities of “This Great City,” and
sundry other shows are getting over the dull time
between now and Boxing Night as quickly as
possible.
Some Hamlets like the tempest rage,
And nightly howl around the stage,
Whilst others play the sighing sage
And scold their mothers gently ;
But one adopts the simple plan
Of acting like a blunt young man
Who runs a shop or drives a van ;
And this is Walter Bentley.
At Poverty Point.
Walter Silbon says that circus-men, who lead decent
lives, die no sooner than other men.
The favourite book of Major Rae, the dwarf with Sells’
Circus, is “ Looking Backward." He grows quite
eloquent w'hen discussing it. He thinks Rider Haggard’s
works are most pernicious for children and young women.
He read “ She ” and “ King Solomon’s Mines,” and then
abandoned Rider Haggard, “for he would make a man
dotty.”
Sells’ contortionists are simply freaks of nature. They
are “ born limber,” and never strain themselves or get
sore. Rexo tells a yarn of a man who, “ born limber,”
never saw a contortionist until 20 years of age, and who,
on his first attempt, executed nearly all the human serpent’s
twists. The folks at Rexo and Reno’s boarding-house
come into their bed-room to see if they sleep in rubber
blankets, they sniff the air for the patent liniment, and
one Sydney athlete offered them £2O for their “snake-
oil ” and then went home and broke a blood-vessel strain-
ing to emulate them. Practice and liniments can no
more develops a contortionist than hair-oils can develop
hair on a bald spot.
Walter Silbon is practising three mid-air somersaults,
and says he will attempt this apparently impossible feat
in public in about 12 months’ time.
The way to touch the circus-elephant s soul is to whistle
down his trunk. One day, in America, a Britisher com-
menced to whistle “Britons,” &c. “Mac” coiled his
trunk up, and slinging it out against the blower’s chest
stretched him senseless on the earth.
Work, regular feeding, and work, are the main factors
in taming the performing elephant. His salary is a
couple of pounds of sweets paid nightly after coming off.
His cue is the elephant-hook, which, from the audience,
appears to he a toy-whip scarce large enough to whip a
cat. But the thong conceals a spike and hook each two
inches in length. The spike, when stuck into the trunk or
into the body behind the near fore leg, “makes him caper,
by criky.” The hook, when yanked into the tender spot,
behind the ear, brings the great beast down on his knees,
and makes him whimper and tremble like a convulsed
infant, The motto taught the elephant by his trainer is :
good performance—sweets ! Bad performance—elephant-
hook !
Baby Stirk, only three years of age, already earns her
bread and butter—that is, her milk-sop and sugar, on her
diminutive bicycle.
In the great aerial feat of the Silbons the strain on th e
partner who catches the other is terrible. Fancy hang-
ing by the legs, head-down, and catching by the hands a
man of 1641b5. weight, after he has swung from a distance
of 74ft. and turned two somersaults 1
When Allan Sells was asked if he punished the hippo-
potami so as to make them so tractable, he replied—“ No,
sir, handling them since they were babies is the way we
tamed them. Our animals are too valuable to be knocked
about. It’s only when we come along to your country, and
you kill our horses for us, and quarantine our infected
animals along with your healthy ones in the Zoo that they
suffer.” The hippopotami are fed on anything in the
vegetable-line—mostly grain and chopped feed.
The Arab Hercules, who bears up seven men at Sells’
Circus, is of splendid physique. He measures 52 inches
round the expanded chest, and 16 inches round the
biceps. His muscles have that springy feel that one
rarely finds in big men. The giant goes slow in the
sword duel now. At one time, when he was set flourish-
ing his weapon to music, he was apt to put in a “ rtd-
hot-’un ” now and again so as to impart a little zest to the
game.
Darmont’s gruesome play, “Pauline Blanchard,” has
been a failure in America.
That £20,000 legacy of Magee’s has already doubled
itself —in print. He is now said to be going “ home” to
claim £40,000.
A Melbourne weekly refers to G. C. Miln as the son of a
parson, and remarks that the fact is apparent in his
elocution. Miln himself was the parson, one of the most
popular and biggest-salaried preachers in America, and
when he walked out of the pulpit to play Hamlet, at the
head of his own company, a great crowd of fashionable
forsaken sheep rolled up to cheer the shepherd on his
way. Church and Stage have never come in closer con-
tact than on the night of Miln’s first appearance as a
play-actor. But he isn’t of parsonic descent or theatrical
extraction by 7 any means. The bohemian strain in Miln’s
too full supply of blood came no doubt from his mother,
the daughter of a gifted but now forgotten litterateur—
“ Christopher North”—who wrote Times leaders in that
paper’s halcyon days.
This is how a dramatic critic liar exposes his fraudulent
hand in a Sydney evening paper. After a few intro-
ductory sobs of sympathy for bad business all-round, he
devotes himself separately to each show-house and ex-
plains: “ ‘ Evangeline ’ is booming along,” “ The Criterion
is crammed nightly,” “Jenny Lee has every reason to be
satisfied with receipts,” “‘Youth’ continues to draw
packed houses/’ “ Stanley 7 has had a phenomenal run.”
Now, what is a bewildered public to believe?
Last Saturday morning, while practising, one of Sells’
trapeze acrobats missed his hold, fell into the net, bounced
out of it on to the ground—a fall of ten feet—and sprained
his back and neck very seriously 7.
Anyone acquainted with the amateur athlete is aware
of his pitiable self-denial while training. Sells’ circus
contortionists, always in splendid buckle, eat what they
fancy, and drink any thing in moderation. One smokes,
on an average, 12 cigars a day ; the other smokes cigar-
ettes incessantly.
Hadj Le Mahafode Cherin, the priest of the Arabs with
Sells’ Circus, is a good soul. When we entered the dress-
ing-room he was squatting—for that is the correct pose
of the contrite Moslem —and his glittering eye was fixed
on eternity. He told us afterwards that he was praying
when we entered, and that he didn’t stop for anybody,
not even for us. He prays five times a day regularly, and
washes himself from head to foot prior to each act of
devotion. We pass this leaf out of the Koran along to
General Booth. All the “submerged tenth” that we
have come into collision with struck us as wanting sub-
merging very badly 7.
Jennie Opie, who has achieved such a success with
Williamson’s Opera Co., is a native of Moonta (S.A.)
Moonta is famous for producing good vocalists ; the popu-
lation is mostly 7 Cornish.
Siraonsen’s Opera Co. appeared at Adelaide Royal under
disadvantageous circumstances. All the dresses and
scenery—what there was of the latter—were left behind
at Serviceton by the railway people, and they had to
appear in evening dress the first night. There was a big
row over the matter, and Italian profanity was very pre-
valent on the Saturday evening.
Lectures don’t go down in Brisbane. When Charles
Clark was there he often had to apologise for not being a
circus or a variety-show, as the Brisbane bald-heads
stumped out for their nine o’clock snake-juice. A lecture
to the Brisbaneite is a one-act performance. Five acts is
the lowest endurable limit in the thirsty North.
In his matchless serial feat Walter Silbon starts his
swing from a cross-bar 74ft. distant from his partner,
Herbert, and after leaving the swing traverses 18ft. while
turning the double-somersault. His hands must come
within 6in. of those of his partner, who, suspended on
another swing, eatohes him. Steadiness of nerve is all-
essential. Young men of dissolute habits, listen to Mr.
Silbon on smoking and be advised. “Regularly I smoke
ten cigars a day and two bunches of cigarettes.” Also all
intoxicants stronger than whisky are religiously eschewed
by him, for Mr. Silbon says: “Drink stronger than
w hisky completely undermines a man’s nervous system,
and when a man’s nervous system is done, the man’s
done. Black coffee in moderation is good for the wind.”
It took five years of constant practice—from 10 to 30
attempts every day—to enable him to perform his won-
drous feat at all.

audience at Manchester who stood on chairs and howled
until P. had played them his own minuet.

A. M. Denison, the actor who lately committed suicide
in New York, will be known to many of the profession in
Australia as a good fellow 7 , and a capable man. He has
been attached to the Kendals’ company during their last
three tours in America, where he was very popular. His
acting and make-up as an old Tory sw 7 ell could not be ex-
celled. In “ Aunt Jack ” he was the very counterpart in
manner of Lord Augustus Loftus in his best mood.

Edward Terry, writes our London correspondent, has
fairly “struck oil” with Pinero’s new 7 play, “The Times ”
It is considered the best thing the author has done, and
will prove, like “Sweet Lavender,” a little gold-mine to
the lucky actor-manager. The bookings are enormous,
and seats can’t be had before the end of November.
London Star made a serious mistake the other day in
asserting that the Countess Cadogan, potential mother-
in-law to one of the Royal Princesses, was in the habit of
disporting herself on the stage as a super. They confused
her with an elderly, gay, fiaxen-haired lady who is a
conspicuous figure at all race meetings on Newmarket
Heath, where she follows the start of every event. This
frivolous old party was stage-smitten, and very frequently
made her appearance at various London theatres to the
great scandal of her friends. She is not the only mem-
ber of the aristocracy who has the same mania. The wife
of Lord de , one of the oldest titles in the peerage,
whilst her husband was away in America shooting, used
every night to drive from her house in Park Lane to the
London Avenue, in her smart brougham, and disport
herself in tights amongst the coryphees. She is a splen-
didly-formed woman, and is naturally proud of the fact,
and on the stage she was the cynosure of neighbouring
eyes. The matter got so talked about that her husband
gave up his trip and returned hurriedly to London to
insist upon the discontinuance of her freaks.
Zerbini, the cleverest Melbourne musico, ceased playing
his earthly viola the other day. Unparalleled on the
rumbo-thrumbo, brilliant on the piano, excellent on the
organ, he was what they called a double-handed musician,
rather eminent even in London, which he would not have
left hut for his health. He was a big, fat man, with an
everlasting look of far-away, dreamy benevolence, as he
munched a cigarette and beamed through Iris specs.,
while his mind weltered in a new op. for the string quar-
tette, which he popularly bossed—a more than decent
fellow.
England V. N.S. Wales.
A tremendous crowd rolled up on Saturday last
at Sydney Association ground to see the cricket match
between Lord Sheffield s team and N.S.VV. The grand-
stand and lawn were choke-full, the outside ground was
occupied to the last inch, the members’stand was crowded,
and the ladies overflow ed from their reserve and invaded
the domains of the men in the upper-gallery. The great
attraction was Grace. “ Which is him?” was the query
one heard as soon as people got into the ground. He has
got no older than when he was here half a generation ago,
hut he is a tremendous lot fatter. He is a very big, powerful
man with a bristly black beard nearly to his waist, some-
what slanting eyes, great muscular arms and huge hands.
And, Great Scott—such feet ! He could get two
pounds a week and his tucker merely to walk about
in the grasshopper districts to kill off the pest.
He bats as well as ever, his eye being ns true and his arm
as strong as in the days of old ; but when it comes to
bow ling, he is a bit off. He rolls up to the crease with a
lumbering action like a Clydesdale colt and delivers the
ball with a cunning spin that wants watching, but he is
not dangerous to careful batsmen. When fielding he
stands point, where he does not have to run, and any ball
within possible reach is sure to And a resting-place in one
of his vast, carpet-bag-like hands.
# * #
Next in interest to Grace was Lord Sheffield.
He is a little, fat, stumpy man for all the world like an
English fanner of the old standard type. Hair long and
straggly,* lips and face roughly shaven, and a little fringe
of beard left under the chin, eyes small and cute ; thick-
necked, heavy-jowled, obstinate, good-natured, and
shrewd, his Lordship is just the sort of man that would
make a fine landlord for a bush pub. All readers of the
“Golden Shanty” will remember the picture of Mr.
Michael Doyle, the landlord of the lonesome pub., on the
outside of the book. Well, that picture is as good a like-
ness of Lord Sheffield as any man can want. It could
hardly be improved on. And all far-downers, cockatoos,
sw'aggies, shearers, rouseabouts, and cornstalks generally
are hereby informed that if they want to know what a
real live English lord is like, they should buy the “Golden
Shanty” and study the picture on the outside.—[Advt.
P.S.— We can here see melud buying the “Golden Shanty'.”]
To return to our mutton, his Lordship—he is a great
“sport,” taking a lively interest in, and spending a heap of
money over it. He is’ said to be very averse to women,
and will not have one near his place. Yet he can’t
keep outside the influence of lovely woman, for
it is on record that, on the voyage out, the
beauteous “ Witch of Atlas,” w'ho travelled in the same
ship, one day (wdthout his knowledge), for a wager,
“ willed ” him into the ship’s smoking-saloon, and into
that saloon he went in no time. It is also recorded that
the witch has willed that he shall leave Australia
unmarried —which at once settles the matter. It is hardly a
great ambition for one of England’s noblemen to spend his
money and his time carting a cricket team about the
world. Still, it is better than many things other noble-
men do, so w'e must not complain. He sat in the mem-
bers’ pavilion all the time, quite absorbed in the game.
* # *
Jersey was there hovering round Sheffield like
an amiable flamingo. Also, George Dibbs and George
Reid w andered about together in the most amicable way,
raising some cheering and some chaffing from the crowd.
The crowd, by the way, are none too fond of seeing then-
own men go out. When Alick Bannennan was given out,
a bit of a howl was raised though no one outside the
enclosure could possibly tell whether the decision was
right or wrong ; again, when Iredale was given out, being
palpably caught by the bowler, another hoot was raised.
This sort of thing savours very strongly of the win, tie or
wrangle school, and should be put down sharply. The
game itself was as dismal as ever. The only thing that
redeemed its intolerable dulness was that the wickets
were bad, so the stonewallers got out quicker than
usual. Even then the New South Wales men took
four hours to make 132 runs, being at the rate of
33 runs an hour. Massie and Bonnor used to score at the
rate of 70 runs an hour,and the difference in watching
the two games can be imagined. The stonewallers are
killing cricket, and a good job, too. At present it is a
game that a nation should be ashamed to play, a game
without risk, without dash, without excitement, and only
needing that kind of skill which is developed by the spot-
stroke billiardist who keeps on monotonously potting the
red. The spot-stroke has been barred to make the game
of billiards worth watching, and the stone-walling will
have to be barred in some way to make cricket worth
watching. The very bowlers have given up bowling at
the wickets, and now bowl far to the off to try and tempt
men to hit One trembles to think what the afternoon
would have been like on Saturday if the wickets had been
true and if Bannennan and Donnan had got fairly set. It
is time that crick; t either livens up or gives way to some-
thing else. In the end the Englishmen won by four
wickets, the respective scores being N. S. Wales first
innings, 74 ; second, 172 ; English Eleven, first innings, 94;
second, 6 wickets for 155.
Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are—land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each
individual to live upon, but it has been left to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co. to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of a cool chamber on the hottest day or
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
works at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Send for particulars or inspect the machines work-
ing in our offices. T. A. Knight and Co., St.
James Chambers, King-street, Sydney.

of the N.S.W. Civil Service.

Federation and interprovincial Freetrade. Aus-
tralian Federation will lead to the Australian
Republic.

its members of trying to sell the vote of the party
to Dibbs in exchange for the portfolio of Minister
of Industry.
The N.S.W. Parliament allows the hang-
man and the gaol-flogger a vote each, but denies
a vote to the pauper who has failed to supply the
hangman with a job.

Wales Government for breach of agreement in

And if not, why not ?

are to save the Queensland sugar industry has
duly arrived to the number of 331 all told ; or 271
working bachelor adults, 21 married pairs and 18
children. God bless the new curse !

They’re not the backbone, but the bingey.
At nine o’clock one night last week, a
N.S.W. politician was incapably drunk in a
Sydney hotel. The House sat late, and the mem-
ber revived sufficiently to deliver an able and
argumentative speech. A late sitting has its
advantages after all.
Ninian Melville has introduced a Bill pro-
viding for free education in N.S.W. This is cue
of the many respects in which N.S.W. has lagged
behind Victoria. To be justly compulsory, edu-
cation must be both secular and free. In N.S.W.
it is neither secular nor free.

ment and the squatting Parliament.”

Bruce Smith has been in high glee since
he read a casual reference to his “Liberty and

politician once made copious quotations from tins
book in Parliament House; but Bruce Smith did
not then ostentatiously claim the incident as “a

the influence of his political writings.” Is it a
fact that the book has been withdrawn from sale
in Sydney ?

Premier Playford says. that since the
establishment of Responsible Government in
South Australia in ’s6—which brought into play
the One-man-one-vote principle in that province -
several attempts have been made to alter the Con-
stitution in various directions, but there has never
been evinced the slightest desire to alter or abolish
the One-man-one-vote. That is a sufficient
answer to all talk about the system being “ an
experiment” and a “ dangerous innovation.”
“ It seems necessary to point out to dull
and stupid restrictionists that the oath of alle-
giance refers to the Queen personally and her
heirs, and not merely to the Crown, and any man
who has publicly expressed his belief in Repub-
licanism is guilty of something very like perjury
in subsequently taking the oath,” says the Sydney
Cuckoo. Well, at present Australians have before
them two alternatives—(l) treason to Australia,
(2) perjury. Let us, therefore, commit enforced
perjury.
Premier Dibbs is to be asked to call upon
Monty Arnold, State-pensioner, broker, and
investor, either to resume his old position of Clerk
of the Select Committees, or to forego his pension.
He got his pension on the alleged ground of
suffering from an incurable malady—-but notwith-
standing this “malady” he is able to supervise
the investment of his capital, carry on a large
broking-business, direct the noxious National
Association, and run for Parliament when there’s
a vacancy in the representation of the family seat.
The week before last the old, clammy
Argus came out with two distinct howls. The
first was a demand for Free Speech, and a yell
against certain alleged individuals who, it was
claimed, desired to smother the monopolistic
“National” Association. The second, which
appeared next day, was a hoarse bark of rage
against certain municipal authorities who had
allowed their town-halls to be used for meetings to
advocate the abolition of plural-voting, that not
being the variety of Free Speech the Argus was
pining after.
“The Freetrade party is the party of
economy.” On the very day upon which Parkes
resigned, leaving behind him an empty Treasury
and a mountain of debt, he wrote with his own
hand and sent to England to his old friend,
sculptor Woolner, an order for three statues, to
adorn Sydney public offices, at 3000 guineas
apiece! No particulars concerning the size-, &e.,
of the statues appear in this extraordinary
order, and it will be interesting to learn whether
the details of the matter were entrusted to Mr.
Oscar Meyer. The order has been countermanded
by cable by Parkes’ successors.
The S.M. Herald should surely be satis*
tied with misrepresenting Toby Barton in its
editorials ; it need not suppress the points of his
Ministerial utterances. The Bulletin is not in a
position to say that the Herald editor or “Mr.
James ” instructed the Herald reporters to sup-
press all the telling passages in Toby Barton’s
scathing exposure of the Parkes Administration.
But it is in a position to say that the report
reflects the utmost discredit on the Herald
reporters. Let a comparison between Hansard
and the Herald decide whether The Bulletin is
not understating the case.
Parkes again boasts that he was 12 years
in Parliament ere he took office. How often was
he asked ? Once. And even then the offer was
not hona-Hde, for it was known he was insolvent
and not in a position to gain re-election. The
fact is that Parkes is a perfect wolf as an office-
seeker and snarls like a hungry tyke when deprived
of his only means of liviny. Look at the old
Anglo-Saxon savage to-day ! At Kiama telling
people from the platform that they “were not
human,” that they were “monkeys,” and “ luna-
tics,” and that they should “go outside and get
drunk and be locked up by the police.’’
Sir John Hay, as president of N.S. VY.
Legislative Council, holds a Crown appointment.
The Civil Service Apt is treated as a dead-letter
in relation to political septuagenarians. John
Smith, a clerk at <£2oo or .£3OO in the Lands-
office, is promptly retired when he passes his
sixtieth year. But Sir John Hay, a rich man, is
permitted to hang on to his billet, though he is
well past his seventieth year, and is, moreover,
physically unable to fill the sinecure he has held
for 20 years at a handsome salary. If a minister
of the crown is incapacitated, his department is
administered by a colleague ; but when Sir John
Hay is incapacitated his department—the Legis-
lative Council—is allowed to go ministerless until
he chooses to die or get well again.

Sydney Evening News —an alleged
people s paper—runs a “society ” paper in which h
apparently prints any articles not palatable to the
mass of its daily readers. In a recent issue of its
autocratic paper it reprints, with marked plea',
sure, a long extract from what it calls “ a '
excellent leader ” on Imperial Federation. The
tone and the trutli of this “ excellent leader” can
be judged by a reference to the election of ‘ki
Labor-members to the N.S.W. Parliament, “jn
Australia,” comments the writer of this “excellent
leader, the excesses of a certain section of tlm

F. W. Ward, ex-editor of Sydney Dailv
Telegraph, who is writing to London Times on
the “ Commonwealth of Australia,” makes a bin-
song about England having trusted her
‘ Southern sons ” “in the loan of many millions
sterling.” This is mixing up business and senti-
ment in the most confounded manner. England
was simply looking for someone to bleed— as she
always is—and she chose Australia. Anon she
choose Argentina, but we don’t hear much about
the “Southern sonship ” of Argentina. Now
when finances are a bit tight with her, and she is
herself being bled, she doesn’t bother about her
/Southern sons,” but tells them to “stop borrow-
ing and remember bow much you owe me.”
Ward writes well, but it is impossible to stand
drivel even when well written.

and, behold ! bis anti-Federation enemy, Reid'

Ibbbs, the Protectionist. Lo ! the ass standeth

4, the Retrenchment or O’Sullivan-party ; 5, the
Federation or Parkes-party ; 6, Mr. Crick ai d
Mr. Willis.
We observe that Mr. George Black, M.L.A.,
according to a Sunday paper, took occasion
to state the other night to his constituents in
West Sydney that he had never been on a Pro-
tectionist paper, and that during bis connec-
tion with The Bulletin he “used all his power
and influence to keep Protection down in it.” In
reply The Bulletin has merely to state (1) that
it is now and has always been a Protectionist
paper of the most uncompromising type; (2) that
Mr. Black’s presence or absence never made any
difference in the policy of The Bulletin; (3)
that the editor never enquired and never knew
Mr. Black’s exact fiscal opinions which were of no
more consequence to The Bulletin than the fiscal
opinions of any other person who had no power to
commit the paper.
Filibuster Stanley having, accord ng to his
own brag, prospected South Africa in the name of
the Loar’d for all the European thieves who want
to not only steal the black man’s land, but also
steal and enslave the nigger himself, “ has strong
views on the black-labour question.” Natural
this, on the part of the man whq has irresponsibly
lorded it over so much black or brown humanity.
So he fells a Sydney reporter that Northern Aus-
tralia ought to be run by—no, not by, but on-
black labour. And why, think you ? Because
(italics are our own ) :
The people who are most interested, the people who
own the land up there, who have invested their money in
it, should be allowed to say what they will do with their
country.
Their country ! The people who own the land are
the most interested? The governing classes again!
A few years ago the Victorian farmer, who
now whoops so loudly for the capitalist and .two
votes, arrived on his selection with a lame horse
and a tip-dray loaded with a broken plough and
several children, and according to bis unimpaired
vision, no man had a right to hold more then 320
acres. After practically giving away the land,
the Government, by building railways and grant-
ing every concession asked for, have placed him
in an independent position, and with the aid of
the bank ho can keep on continually increasing
his area, which lie religiously does. Ho will tell
you how, by bis own individual effort, he has
made bis farm worth what it is, never admitting
that national progress had anything to do with it.
In most cases it was bis sons who did the work,
and very often Ins daughters, while the old man
sat in the shade and cursed the large landholder.
Truly a man without either land or money is the
only real democrat.

Personal Items.
Lord Sheffield always carries a hair-comb
in his pocket.
Sir Alfred Stephen’s mental condition is
causing considerable anxiety to his family.
Two-thirds of the small gathering round
the grave of Tom Garrett, M.P., were N.S.W.
Lands-office officials.
Bishop Mules is the new episcopus for
Nelson diocese, N.Z. A clergyman is generally
content with being one mule.
Gov. Jersey, as everybody has lately been
informed, was quite a famous runner in his youth.
A sort of scarlet runner, uo doubt.
Tom Bracken, Maoriland’s sweetest poet,
is on a visit to Australia on business connected
with a new volume of his poems just issued.
The Czar of Russia is the best paid of
European sovereigns. He takes home .£20,000
every week to the Missus in his hot little hand.
Lord Jersey says : “ The name of Stanley
will endure while the world lasts.” Thus does
“.Rowlands,” as a cognomen, escape all-time
record.
Joe Thompson is at his old “flash”
games. He turned up at the late Doncaster (Eng.)
settling with his clothes stuffed with £50,000, all
in notes.
The insufferably-advertised Mrs. Frank
Leslie—now Mrs. Frank Leslie Wilde—has been
three times married. She was a divorcee when
she wedded Leslie.
Mrs. Bernard-Beere claims to be the god-
daughter of W. M. Thackeray. That may be as
may be ; one thing is pretty certain —Mrs. B. was
qnce at London Gaiety bar.
Judge Redmond Barry-once threatened to
commit barrister Byron Miller. “Should you do
so, I won’t be the worst thing your Honor has
committed,” retorted Miller.
W. G. Grace, the Jumbo of the English
team, is very fat. His waddle up to the wicket
when bowling suggests that he learnt his paces in
the same school as Sells’ hippopotami.
More in-breeding. It now seems pretty
certain that Albert Victor is to marry his cousin
Victoria of Teck. Better for the house of Guelph
that its heir should elope with and marry the
cook.
Ward, Lock, Bowden and Co. sold, within
a year, 30,000 copies of “ Digger Dick’s Darling,?
a collection of short stories by Mrs. Blitz,
formerly of Townsville, Q., now of Tamworth,
N.S.W.
During a recent debate in N.S.W.
Assembly, Mr. Newman, M.L.A., said that he
had taken out 20 tons of gold from where Toms
and Lister first found the metal in payable
quantities.
Lord Augustus Loftus soon tired of
Bruges, and he is back at Leatherhead, where
young Gussy, who still takes shelter under the
paternal wing, can indulge in his favourite hobby
of chicken-rearing.
j||Lord Sheffield has a quiet unostentatious
way cf doing things. He instructed Shaw, his
manager, to find out the names of all the Adelaide
charities, and the day before he left Adelaide
cheques were sent all round.
David Jones, the big Sydney rag-man, has
amalgamated with Thompson, of Thompson and
Giles. While Jones and Thompson were amal-
gamating they might just as well have taken in
Smith and Brown, also Robinson.
A Bathurst (N.S.W.) editor makes his
rotund and hated rival go to a tailor for a suit,
at £3. The tailor introduced his tape. “Forty,
fifty, sixty, seventy,” and then he stopped.
“Sir,” said he, “for £3 I cannot go further.”

quest re the London Gaiety Theatre chorus-girl

Hence the meagre particulars published of the
giddy girl’s relations with certain effete Per-
sonages.

please his wife.” From what has been heard of

fished solicitor in Sydney was appointed Post'
master-General, the news astounded the clerks in
the office, one of whom lost his billet by exclaim-
ing : “ Good Heavens ! It will take him an hour
to write a minute !”.
Captain Maun, the Victorian Naval Com-
mandant who insisted on “full beards, or a clean
shave,” is retiring in favor of Captain White, au
Imperial officer of about five and forty. White’s
ideas on the subject of hair will be duly announced
when he arrives in March next.
Sir Henry Parkes was once carefully
examining a Bulletin cartoon of himself. A
Colonial Secretary’s Office official courageously
remarked that the cartoon was awfully ugly.
“You may think so,” sniffed the ancient politi-
cian, “ but my wife thinks it insufficiently ugly.”
Mr. Matthias Larkin took a pretty long
“ trip”—he has been robbing his building society
for the last four years. And, by the way, he left
off posting up the ledgers sometime in 1889, a
circumstance which goes to show that a board of
directors is a useful tiling to have about an office,
anyway.

Innaniincka re-named by him “ Hopetoun ” as a
compliment to his friend the Victorian Governor,
is again called by its native appellation. Kintore
says he didn’t know anything about the native
name, at which the Adelaide people ejaculate
“ Walker!”
Mrs. Major Rae, Sells’ charming little
midget, on matrimony: “People should not bo
sentimental before marriage ; after marriage is the
proper time to be sentimental.” She has been
married only ten months. The tiny woman, like
all her sex, “loves all sentimental books.” She
reads her Bible regularly. “Beulah” is her
favourite work.
Jamieson, quondam surveyor, one of the
lucky Broken-Hill Proprietary men, is working
his way up to being aA English country magnate,
and has a delightful estate in the vicinity of
Salisbury. He was recently married, and is
understood to have previously declined a chance of
joining his big fortune with the mine-made pile of
a heavily-gilded Australian widow.

Vie. Assembly the other night, and desired to

A very good portrait of Dr. Perry, first
Bishop of Melbourne, just deceased, is preserved
in the National Gallery. Nobody has been com-
missioned to put the last bishop (Goe) on canvas,
a matter which wants .seeing, to without delay.
As a specimen of what bishops could occasionally
look like in the last days of proud prelatical institu-
tions, Goe’s picture would make future genera-
tions sit up and rub their eyes.
Sir Henry Parkes has always been a fond
admirer of lovely woman. So long ago as 1851,
when he wore no hair on his face, ho was called
upon to toast “The Ladies of x\ustralia.” “I
pine at this moment,” he stuttered, “for some
power of eloquence, some word of electric might
and influence, to speak out the fervour of my
admiration and the depth of my reverence for the
lofty and angelic character of a virtuous woman.”
Precise morning paper cablegram :
It is officially announced that Prince George of Wales
has reached the convalescent stage of ins illness.
Fancy having all that officially flashed per official
cable! Presumably George has been only
officially ill. And he can’t get well without official
sanction. And he’ll never die until he officially
pegs out. We feel officially tired of this official
slush.
An Adelaide ’Tlser reporter who inter-
viewed Rudyard Kipling was told by that fashion-
able lounger, as he sipped his light wine and
breathed out his fragrant cigar, that what the
working man in Australia wanted was 15 hours
hard work a day. Kipling was born and bred in
India, among niggers, and was probably wet-
nursed by a nigger woman. He is a nigger-driver
and has no business olf the chain in a white man’s
country.
After all, the Divine Sara is not a
thoroughbred Frenchwoman. Worse than all, the
cross is a German one. Someone has fished out
her certificate of birth, from which it appears that
the Queen of Tragedy is the daughter of a Berlin
Jewess, who was employed as a sempstress in the
Quartier Latin, Who her father was is not said.
Sara owes her education to a rich financier, who
sent her to a convent at Versailles and defrayed
the cost.
An effusive Tasmanian lady is very wroth
with the Hobart Mercury for libelling Albert
Edward in a cable. Lord Salisbury was reported
to have “regretted the Prince of Wales no longer
Wore unspotted the robe of royalty.” The error is
excusable if the sub-editor received, as probably
lie did, the words —“Salisbury regretted Wales
no longer wore unspotted robe.” “Wales” was
New South Wales, and the “ unspotted robe ” was
the robe of Freetrade.
Alfred Deakin is more than likely to enter
a large legal firm in Melbourne, in consequence' of
the passage of the Legal Profe sions Amalgama-
tion Bill in Victoria, and, if certain negotiations
are concluded, he will retire from politics
and undertake all the Court work of that
firm. There is money in Alf.’s silveiy voice and
his rhythmic poweis of persuasion, and he is
getting more nondescript-ly Liberal in politics
every day. Agk doth work miracles, as the
Symist David might say.
Old Sir Frederick Whitaker, ex-Prcmier
of N.Z., who died the other day, was called the
Nestor of the Maoriland Parliament. He had
been 50 years in the colony, and from 184 G until
his death was connected with public affairs. He
was a queer-looking little man who wore decidedly
baggy pants, a shapeless coat, and a single eye-
glass. He had a long, thin, forensic-looking nose
and as keen an eye as ever shone out of human
skull. He had several sons, but the brightest of
all, Fred., who was also in Parliament, com-
mitted suicide some years back.
The S. M. Herald endorses Braconnier’a
encomium upon Stanley as a man who was in-
variably kind, merciful, and polite towards th e
African natives, but unutterably inconsiderate
and selfish as regards his own officers, “ready at
any moment to sacrifice any one of them to his
own safety,” arrogant, overbearing and unreason-
able. “ I have watched him,” quotes the Herald
from Braconnier, “smoking under his own tent
knowing all the time his officers had no tobacco,
and it would never occur to him to offer them a
pipe.” What a damning indictment! It is
evident Stanley is a true Exter Haul Christian,
and that as his friendly critic says, “You must
live a long time with him to understand him.”
Most people know of Gulgong, N.S.W.,
once the scene of a historic rush, and the centre of
a population numbering 100,000. A strange relic
of those feverish days, a woman locally known as
“ Cranky Mary,” the whilom sweetheart of one of
the tars of the Franklin Relief Expedition, now
strays about the district. The poor woman’s lover
was killed by the caving-in of a shaft, and the
bereavement blighted her reason. Carrying all
her property in a swag, and wearing her lover’s
Franklin medal on her neck, she begs her way
from station to station, from humpy to humpy.
When in need of rest, she pitches her tent, lays
her blankets on the ground, and “camps” until
sufficiently refreshed to resume her aimless wan-
derings.
Send sevenpence in stamps for the Xmas
Bulletin, published on Thursday, 17th December.
It will contain 20 bright stories in verse and prose,
all written on Australian soil.
Some native officers of the Calcutta Geolo-
gical Department recently applied for a holiday
on the straight ground that they were “suffering
from perspiration and a want of enthusiasm for
work.”
“ Very much intoxicated, but quite
capable,” is the latest police-court definition of
drunkenness. It comes from Brisbane and is the
sort of definition which enabled the Sydney police
to arrest Hewart for drunkenness, lock him up in
a cell with another drunkard (whom he killed) and
then have him hanged for the murder, which it
was claimed he committed in his sober senses and
of malice aforethought.
Why is it that death should come before
the allotted age of ‘ three score and ten,’ and why
so many millions of human beings never live to
three score and five, and why that not one in
every thousand lives to three score years? We
kill ourselves. Many interesting particulars are
found in tables of physiologists and writers on
hygiene. The eminent physiologists, Haller and
Buffon, present interesting particulars on the
subject of longevity. They treat it in two ways,
historical and physical. The historical side is the
recitation of all facts known of the naturally
ordinary and extreme duration of life, and the
physical aspect of the problem involves the con-
templation of the great natural phases of develope-
ment of the species, digestion, period of growth
and length of life.
Mankind pays too little attention to the warn-
ing voice of nature. Signs of approaching calamity
are unheeded, the disorganization of the affected
organ increases and after a time becomes insen-
sible, then no pain is felt and the sufferer is
deluded into the belief that he has shaken off the
disease. Nothing could be more erroneous or
dangerous, for the complaint has only passed on
the further stage towards the end and nature
ceases to send those warning aches and pains as
they were unnoticed when sent. The debility and
weakness increases however, and nervousness, loss
of appetite, and general languor comes on, and a
cold is caught in the kidneys, lungs or liver, and
another life is sacrificed to Bright’s disease, Con-
sumption, or some other serious and fatal disease.
Scoffers can talk as they like at the virtues claimed
for Clements Tonic, but even the most prejudiced
men know it is genuine and does possess merits
no other medicine ever did, as instanced in the
following case. “ For the past four years I have
been a great sufferer from severe pains in the back
and left side, being at times quite unable to do
any work or even walk about. I suffered in this
way until it became quite unbearable and in
February, 1889, I consulted a physician who
treated me for seventeen months, and during that
time I suffered the severest torture under his
treatment, and instead of improving, continued to
get worse, he said at last, ‘ I have done all I can
and you will never be better.’ This drove me to
despair and I went under another doctor’s treat-
ment for four months and took about 30 bottles of
medicine, but with no result. I was giving up all
hopes when I read of the wonderful cures effected
by Clements Tonic, and on my husband’s advice I
tried it, and after the use of only three bottles I
was quite a different woman. I have taken 15
bottles and feel as well and strong as ever, and
can do my work with ease and pleasure, whereas
it was a great trouble before using Clements
Tonic. I can now also walk for miles without
fatigue. Before taking this medicine I have often
suffered with Neuralgia for weeks at a time and
could never get any relief, but since taking
Clements Tonic, I have never had an attack of it
since, as a result of ray experience I have much
pleasure in recommending it to all afflicted. Mrs.
S. E. Baker, Swan-street, Morpeth, N.S.W.”

Society
Whichever way the verdict goes in the
Brisbane cause celebre, there v. ill be an appeal to
the Full Court. Now, Chief Justice Lilley is
hearing the case ; Judge Real is debarred, since,
before his elevation to the Bench, he had been
retained in the case; and Judge Harding is
related by marriage to one of the defendants.
Does the last-named fact bar Harding ? If so,
can Lilley sit as a Full Court by himself ? Then
will Lilley as a Full Court be a satisfactory critic
upon the judgment of Lilley as a simple Court ?
Suppose Lilley in his Full capacity reversed
judgment, and Lilley in his individual capacity
as a member of the Full Court upheld judgment,
and then Full Lilley, as Full Chairman of the
Full Court, gave a Full casting-vote in favour of a
reversal, accepting the written dissent of Empty
Lilley in his Empty capacity as a member of the
Emptifull Court, why—but we wander, we fancy.
* * *
It is understood in Brisbane that the Fnil
Court did not meet last week on its regular day,
because the fourth son of the Chief Justice chose
that particular Wednesday for getting married.
In other words—of this be so, and certainly two
Supreme'Court Judges were at the wedding—the
business of suitors, to attend to which the Judges
are handsomely paid, must be postponed, no
matter at what cost and inconvenience to said
suitors, because a lovesick young man feels that
he must be, like Polly Pluck, “married on a
Wed-nes-dee.”
* * *
It would appear that the fashionable
audience which, within the last 12 months,
attended a certain Australian theatrical wedding,
merely assisted at a theatrical bigamy.
* * •
As there seems tc be some difficulty anent
the British jurisdiction in the Melba divorce-case,
the unhappy pair ought to come to Australia and
fight out the issue. It would be a godsend to the
evening papers, a syndicate of the proprietors of
which would, no doubt, be ready to co-operate
with J. C. Williamson as to the season’s expenses.
* * #
The conjugal life of an Australian actress
will, it is said, soon form the subject of an enquiry
in the Divorce Court. A year and a half ago this
lady married a glittering personage whose father,
long since dead, amassed a large fortune as a
speculator. The marriage was denied at first by
the actress’s guardian, but certain alleged truths
have leaked out in an alleged sort of way, and the
wealthy husband’s people are resolved that the
marriage shall be annulled.

Much fuss has been made about requiring
Jews to vote on Saturday; but, as a matter of
fact, the Rabbi in Sydney says that no Hebrew is
precluded by his persuasion from discharging his
■duties* as a citizen, on the Jewish sabbath ; and
for his part he would have no hesitation in record-
ing his parliamentary vote on a Saturday.

# • »
To preserve the legal enclosure a little
more strictly, new regulations for LL.B. degree
were introduced at the beginning of the year at
the Adelaide University, doubling the number of
subjects and lengthening the course from three
years to four. Out of the dozen students who
started the course only one managed to get
thmmrh the first year’s examination which has just
finished.
# * #
In S.A. they have a cheerful insolvency
law. Insolvents who are suspected of having
swallowed the silver spoons, or of having buried
the cash-box at midnight, or of having defrauded
their creditors by a fraudulent preference for their
mother-in-law, can be dealt with in three ways.
They can be tried by a jury and sentenced up to
seven years ; or the court can say it is satisfied an
offence has been committed, and can sentence to a
maximum of three years, or the court can say that
it suspects an offence lias been committed, and can
imprison the insolvent until the suspicion fades
away from the mind of the court, which may be
after the death of the bankrupt. One insolvent
has been in prison for three years already under
this suspicion clause, and is no nearer having a
charge laid against him than he was at the
beginning. A private member of the S.A. Par-
liament has brought in a Bill providing that when
■an insolvent has been in prison upon suspicion for
a year, somebody must either lay a charge against
him or else he must be let loose. The Government
and the lawyers of the House strongly oppose the
Bill.

David Syme published in his Illustrated
Australian News two malicious caricatures pur-
porting to be the portraits of Miss Margaret
Whyte and Miss Gracie Clara Stone, the newly-
graduated lady medicoesof Melbourne University.
If the ladies have any male relatives or admirers
on earth the vigilance-bell will ring and a soaped
rope will be ordered for the Age boss.
# # *
Among the details of the big divorce-case in
which the Countess Russell sought to divorce her
husband of but a year and a-half, it is mentioned
that the Countess “had been limited to six
cigarettes a day.” Another sidelight on the
recent!y-developed habits of English society.
* # #
The result of the Russell separation suit,
just concluded in Loudon, is one more illustration
of the wretched incompleteness of the English
divorce laws. The case reeked with nastiness, and,
judging from the necessarily skeletonic narrative
told by the cables, its mainspring appears to
have been that, swayed by the hallucinations
of hysteria, an imaginative woman of low mind
accused her husband of unspeakable offences
intended to make him appear loathsome in the
eyes of man. Countess Russell’s own counsel
publicly withdrew the most horrifying of the
charges. Naturally, she failed in her spit against
Earl Russell. Right here, one would expect some
relief for the husband from the Divorce laws.
Yet, after being accused by his wife of unnatural
offences, he must, according to the laws of Eng-
land, continue to love, honour, and protect the
lady who may, for utter charity’s sake, be
designated “ the patient.”
* * #
The ovation given after the trial to Earl
Russell, by the members of the National Liberal
Club was either a demonstration of thankfulness
at a man’s escape from an hysterical woman, or
else a jubilate because a member of the aristocracy
had proved that he was not an irredeemable black-
guard.

# * #
A well-known mining expert travels on
the fact that he takes his fee, but never anything
more. However, it has just transpired that his wife
accepts big bundles of scrip, while the guileless
expert calmly winks the other eye.
# # #
The wife of the “ world’s greatest ex-
plorer ” — vide Smythe—is one of the best of
women, for she believes in her husband. Bless
the women ! They are mostly the same, right down
to the poor Sydney drudge who, with broken nose
and shipwrecked aspect, tried to beg her husband
off last week after he had culminated a 10 years’
wickedness by disfiguring her for life. The un-
happy woman swore she did it herself -that she
was disgracefully drunk and fell against the
table. To try and save him she imperilled herself
and her immortal soul with the utterance of a
false mca culpa. No one can but say “Eheu !
elien ! ” The story is told in manifold pages of
Dickens’ novels. The faith of many women in their
husbands is implanted like the heart in cabbages.
# # *
A wedding in high life was celebrated at
Mackay, Q., quite recently, with unusual Mat.
The principal sacrifices and their friends wanted
the “ Wedding March ” at any price, but were too
loftily-minded to ask the lady organist. Higher
church officers always referred them back to the
organist, but pride prevailed, and the “Wedding
March ” was eventually tumtytwceblared outside
the church door, by a piano, a violin, and a
cornet, amidst the jeers of the wicked and the
perverse, assembled to see the two victims hand-
cuffed for life. Mackay is invincible when it’s in
earnest.
» * • -
Mr. “ Beehive ” Benjamin, one of Mel-
bourne’s undemonstrative millionaires, is on the
point of taking a second plunge into matrimony.
If the lady, who is comely and not too giddy-
girly, can persuade the happy man to celebrate
his nuptials by a few fits of munificence —such as
a joyous reduction of rents on his city properties
—she will do him a great service. At the present
moment Benjamin is far too rich to feel certain of
salvation, although he wears the white waistcoat
of a blameless life. Nobody seems to have any
tales to tell of how Benjamin (no relation of Sir
Bingy) met him in the street, in the hour of need
and pressed a bank note into his trembling hand.
As a convivialist he is not equal to the average
Hebrew, and he charges his army of tenants a
per cent, or two a week without attempting to
make them enjoy the joke. However, Cupid
may, perhaps, start this prudent embodiment of
biicks and mortar on a new and wild career of
prodigality, by opening his eyes to the fact that
he owes Melbourne a great heap of unearned
increment, notwithstanding that he made his first
start with a small capital and a small consignment
of walnuts on a barrow. May the torch of Hymen
warm his heart at this Merry Xmas time !

A young man about Sydney town had an
unhealthy two minutes the other night on leaving
the theatre. A little super who fancied she had at
least secured a perfect gentleman who really did
love her, shammed sick on seeing him sole escort
of a soulful maiden, went off, drank a glass or two
of brandy, waited for him at the dress-circle
entrance and showed him up badly.

• * •
A certain Brisbane solicitor, while on an
idle holiday round New Zealand, met and wooed
a much-admired red-headed angel. In due course
the lady arrives in Sydney on her way to Brisbane
—her trousseau prepared, her clothing all marked
with his initials. She waited, and at last received
a letter from him : “ So sorry, but she had misin-
terpreted his intentions ; he could never love her,
but would release her from her distressing posi-
tion, because it was the only honourable offer he
could make.” The lady declined, and went back
home.

THE STOCKMAN’S GRAVE.
A sighing breeze as the wattles wave,
And a wild, low cry of pain ;
A weird, dank spot round a sandy grave
Unsheltered from sun or rain ;
A stain of blood on the arid ground,
And the sign of a murderer’s ban—
All these in Australian verse I’ve found,
But seldom a living man.
W. A. S.
Item on human ankles, from the London
Globe :
When a woman has ankles almost as thick as the
ordinary human neck, ought she to wear boots or shoes ?
In which do the said ankles look least dreadful ? There is
something to be said on both sides. The polish of the
kid in the boot advertises the liberal girth by attracting
the eye to them ; whereas the dull, sombre tone of black
silk stockings has the effect of diminishing the apparent
size. But then, hoots have a more restraining influence
than shoes, which allow superfluous flesh to boil over
their upper edges in a distracting way. Since woman
took to riding on the tops of onmibuses'the study of the
human ankle has been forced upon the reluctant spectator.
The majority cannot be described as otherwise than de-
pressing. The number of bony ankles is incredible—and
such bones ! They lean over towards each other on their
separate feet in the most aggressive way, and even in
boots print their profile on the outer leather with an
accuracy well worthy of a splendid cause. Pretty, slim
ankles are often seen, it is t»ue, but the proportion they
bear to the ugly ones is infinitesimally small.
* * «
Rev. J. Allen (Benalla, Vic.), determined
to augment the Bishop of Melbourne’s Fund, on
the Booth principle of “ self-denial,” proposes to
lead the van himself. He believes in whisky and
tobacco as God-sent gifts, for, saith he, “good
beer and good whisky are as much the creatures
of God as are good bread and good beef.” He is,
therefore, a “moderate drinker.” He further
believes, as an article of faith, that “tobacco was
created by the good Lord to be the solace of many
wearied and worried men.” Then comes the
challenge to his parishioners to do as he intends
to do, and put the saved moneys into the Bishop’s
Hat: —
But I hereby promise and vow—and all you who hear
me are witnesses—that for twelve months from this
Sunday (if I am spared so long) I will not take any
alcoholic drink as a beverage, and I will smoke no tobacco
in any form, except a little on Sunday nights, unless
specially and peremptorily ordered to do so by a medical
adviser.
There! “As a beverage!” And just a little on
Sunday nights ! Candour and earnestness and
simple mindedness. Come out of the whirl of
shams, Parson Allen.

At the recent Conference of the Women’s
Christian Temperance Union (Melb.) a Mrs.
O’Donnell, “Superintendent of White Cross and
Shield,” called upon her younger and unmarried
sisters to treat the opposite sex with kindness
without being familiar ; also to assist towards the
regeneration ot erring man by wearing longer
dresses.” Which latter suggestion will meet with
wide support. Thousands of husbands and fathers
want to see women wear their dresses longer •
The young Countess of Russell’s yarn
about being compelled to “do menial work ”
sounds like a hysterical delusion, or a mild
exaggeration. Unless cleaning her own teeth
comes under the head of menial labour for a
countess.
* * *
At one of the Melbourne “panic” meet-
ings several gentlemen dwelt upon the fact that
they had deposited trust money with a certain
building society. Surely it must be the trustee
who takes the risk of such an investment, or, if
not, the necessity for mentioning that he is simply
“ showing confidence ” with other people’s money
is scarcely apparent.

At the little sea-coast town of New
Brighton (M.L.) there are long ridges of sand
which have a strange habit of shifting from place
to place, like itinerant dust-heaps or wandering
ash-mounds. They are always rolling, drifting,
and changing; where there is a hill one week
there is a valley the next, and the level spots
climb up to the tops of the ridges, and the hills
climb down on to the level beach again, and every-
thing is uncertain, sad, and transitory. Some-
where about a month ago a family party of five
ladies Went out to bathe in these regions, and, as
it happened, they knew nothing of this local
peculiarity. They utilised a convenient recess
among the sandhills as a dressing-room, and
having climbed into correct bathing costumes,
they went out into the great Pacific and had a
royal time. But when they came out a great sur-
prise fell upon them like a man falling off
a high tower, tor there wasn’t a stitch of
raiment in sight. Neither was there a
single human being on the landscape far
or near, and there was not one solitary
footprint to show where the marauder
had come and gone; and when a distracted
parent in bathing costume climbed up a sandhill
and looked out upon the dry land and the great,
damp sea, there wasn’t a clue on the horizon any-
where. Some of them thought that the clothes
had been carried off by an eagle, though it seemed
eminently improbable that an eagle would have
come back to scrape up the last collar, and to
take away the very last hat and boot, unless he
was an exceedingly economical bird ; another
theory was that the togs had been destroyed by
lightning, and one damp girl, shivering on the
ADVICE TO YOUNG LADIES.
Be kind to the opposite sex,
Yet not too familiar, young misses ;
Take care not to cuddle their necks.
Nor curl their moustaches with kisses.
If mashers get spoony and talk
In a strain that is tender and pleasing,
Then request them to come for a walk
And discuss the cold ethics of squeezing.
Be kind to the opposite sex
And give them a show to make offers
It is best for a girl to annex
A boy who has gold in his coffers.
But when you have answered him “yes,”
And Jack goes to hug his Ameliar,
Why, object to an ardent caress
On the ground that it’s drefful familiar
Be kind to the opposite sex,
For men need your moral assistance,
But give their warm rhapsodies checks,
And bid ’em make love at a distance.
A trim little ankle but mocks
At passionate sighs unavailing,
So add a fresh “ tuck ” to your frocks
Until in the dust they are trailing.
Be kind to the opposite sex,
As Mrs. O’Donnell exhorts you,
Don’c put on a bustle to vex
The soul of the stranger who courts you.
Belong to the White Cross and Shield,'
And frown at the girl who tight laces,
And keep human nature concealed
’Neath a mask of the straightest of faces.

by the hand of Providence, They sat for, at
least, two hours formulating these theories, and at
the end of that period the fat mother of the party
realised that she was sitting on something hard,
and found one of her own boots sticking out of

sand-drift in between. And now they have taken

for the future these ladies do their bathing in a
big wash-basin, and keep their clothes in full view
on a chair all the time.

and howl for the rack, the scavenger’s daughter,

knobbly whipcord in face of the fact that objec-
tionable people used to be crucified after being
hamstrung ?

Mr. Copland, ex-M.L. A. for the Murrum-
bidgee (N.S.W.), runs a Wagga drapery, and
advertises in this desperate fashion :
The world of day-dreams and chivalry have passed awaj',
leaving in their stead business and money-making the
magnets by which men’s minds are seamed.
He who cannot keep pace with the new forces must
fall back in the race, for the survival of the fittest is the
doctrine that holds sway, and is the hard ethical
evolution of the age.
We try to keep in touch with the spirit of the age,
strike the keynote of the people’s tastes, and sell goods
at prices that are in accord with .the times.
We ask you, therefore, to coiile to the Entrepot of the
South, and your soul will be sublimated by the influence
of the beautiful in form and colour.
The stock is as huge as Olympus, the values surpass
.your most cherished day-dreams, and the prices are
without a parallel.
The singularity of the price-list ostracises all other
competitors.
Read the list, the figures will almost asphyxiate you
by their cheapness.
Also, we presume, his pants are as durable as the
gates of perdition, and death will not prevail
against his new hard hats at 7s. fid., assorted
sizes, and his supply of boots reaches up to heaven
and jostles against the Dog-star. Canst thou
draw up leviathan with a hook, or compete
against my new sixpenny tie which fastens by a
buckle under the left ear ?

* # *
At Adelaide, S. A., James Quintin, charged
with escaping from the stockade where he was
serving seven years for burglary, has been sen-
tenced to five years extra in irons. What revolt-
ing judicial savagery ! _ Surely it is the duty of
the State and the gaolers to effectively guard their
prisoners! The love of liberty is the noblest
instinct of man—it is noble even in the most
desperate burglar. If the gaolers can’t secure
Quintin otherwise, let them iron him ; to give him
five years for refusing to bow his head to bondage,
for advantaging himself of the laches of a careless
gaoler, is only worthy of Ashantee—or Botany
Bay.

There is one thing to be said about the
Melbourne building societies up to date—they
seem to have more than 3s. 2d. assets apiece.
# # #
The associated banks of Melbourne found
themselves in a delicate position after they had
allowed one or two building societies to shut up.
In common fairness, the banks couldn’t come to
the rescue of the next institution that found itself
running dry, a dilemma which was, perhaps, not
clearly foreseen.
# * #
L. L. Smith maintains, with pathetic
earnestness, that if Victorians can be induced to
take native wines, instead of beer and spirits,
drunkenness will pass away like a hideous dream.
L. L. has ever been a philanthropist. His ill-
fated Creme-de-Bouzy was brewed in the interests
of sobriety, but it’s meaning was always mis-
understood —especially the next morning, when
the young man “didn’t feel well. ’

The good folks of Geelong, who recently
pelted the Reverend Grubb with jewellery, bank-
notes, and other superfluous vanities, are wanting
to know whether Ins blessed Chinese mission is
coming off under the circumstances. If not, they
would prefer to see the colour of that portable
property once again. They might do a little
private converting with it in their own back-
yards. Any local cabbage-merchant can be lured
into the fold by a diamond ring, or a decent gold
lever. Try the experiment. “ You gi vein ticker
—John turn Clistian to-mollow. Joss no dam
good at allee. Clistian God welly good—you
giveiri John ticker. Allelujah, come to Glorly !”
The conversion of a Chow at a fair price is as
simple as shelling his peas.
# # #
Now that the legal professions have been
amalgamated in Victoria by Act of Parliament,
the old, hard-shell barristers are taking violent
steps to preserve what they call their “status.”
They have formed a solemn league and covenant,
described by themselves as the “ Bar Association
of Victoria,” and by other people, no doubt, as
the Devil’s Ring, the members of which pledge
themselves to have no dealings with any solicitor
who practises as a barrister as provided by the
Act, and not to recognise or consult or associate
in any capacity with any barrister who also acts
as a solicitor, or who accepts briefs from any boy-
cotted solicitor. In other words, the league is an
illegal association, formed to violently abrogate
an Act of Parliament, to interfere with the liberty
of the subject, and to intimidate men while in
pursuit of their lawful calling. Under a certain
Act of George IV., which is always raked up
when workmen instead of barristers practise
intimidation and go on the boycott, a good many
leading legal authorities are now liable to be
seized by the police and sent to break road-metal
in Pentridge, and doubtless that code which hits
the rich and the poor alike will look after them
promptly. Yah !

remains were buried.

It is the practice of a certain bachelor in
the N.S.W. Civil Service to write an erotic epistle,
and sidle up to the fair, with “Excuse me, miss,
but you dropped this.” “No, thank you ; I did
not!” “ But, excuse me ; I saw you drop it.”
Here the girl will take the note—“He has long
loved her, and meet him at 8.” Lately a man
whose intellect has run into muscles and hard
knuckles turned up at the trysting-place. It was
a secluded spot, just the joy of lovers or fighters,
and si our O.S. got laid up with influenza for a
fortnight.

Modern torture. In November, Prof, of
Music Ives, of the Adelaide University, stood
eight hours a day for ten days listening to girl-
candidates at a music examination—playing a
piano. Each girl operated for 20 minutes.
# « *
A wedding was conducted the other day in
Auckland (ML.) under somewhat disadvan-
tageous circumstances. In fact, there was a faded,
wild-eyed woman tearing around the church,
whose presence-was a burden to all parties con-
cerned. She was the bride’s elder sister, and the
bridegroom tiad been married to her—only it was
in a sort of loose, aboriginal manner that didn’t
count. Then, having become engaged to the
younger sister, he threw the elder one out to make
room for her successor, and that was why the dis-
carded woman was sprinting madly round the
church all through the service. It took much
savoir faire to get through with the ceremony.
* * *
The day Sells’ Circus arrived in Sydney,
and within an hour of the arrival, a seedy-looking
parson struck the management for a free-pass.
There were three stern, hard-featured, business-
like men superintending the landing of the pro-
perties, and the haggard preacher hovered round
and round them for a while with an anxious
expression on his countenance. At last he dodged
up to the principal boss of the concern, and said
something in an apologetic whisper in Ins ear, and
that official responded cheerfully, and in a voice
that could be heard half-way down the street:
“ Certainly, sir, certainly, O YES! We’ll pass
you in free with the greatest pleasure. Come right
round any time, and ask for me at the gate.”
There was a capacious grin all down the street,
and the free-pass pastor fled in confusion. A few
days after that we saw the back view of an old,
greenish-black, clerical tail-coat that was hovering
on the stairs of the Theatre Royal; it was
evidently the same parson, and he was plainly in
search of another free-pas-c Last Monday week
we wandered round to hear Stanley, and in front
of us, doddering up the steps of the Centenary
Hall, we encountered the rear aspect of the same
flapping coat—or else its twin brother—and saw
the same thin legs dodging underneath it. The
owner fished out a card and presented it to
the Much-Travelled Smythe, and flapped his
gaunt paws at Smythe, and was evidently
laying the matter fully before him. The
Much - Travelled shook his head, and said
the request was “ most unusual.” Then
the parson’s sad coat - tail flickered more
drearily in the breeze, and his legs took an
entreating aspect, but Smythe stated that the
seats were mostly taken, and tried to go away.
The parson fled round Smythe, and presented
sundry new aspects of the case to him, and pointed
excitedly with his long finger to his card, and
backed the manager up against the wall, but
Smythe only looked reticent. Last of all the
preacher settled down, and talked to Smythe for
ten minutes in one ceaseless flow, as if he had
taken the plug out of a barrel of conversation
and let the contents fly, and at the end of that
tune the great man collapsed. The scene closed
with a seedy expounder entering triumphantly “ on
the never,” and taking a seat as near to the front as
he could get.

Some Bombala (N.S. W.) ladies, who stall-
held at a church-fund flower-show, have been
fined Is. each with 4s. lOd. costs, for (in effect)
gambling with dice, while raffling. Good. Bom-
bala at least attempts to apply the law logically,
and if similar.action were taken in every instance
the folly of trying to suppress gambling'by statute
would soon be recognised.
* • *
In Brisbane, every Sunday morning an
old workman betakes himself to a disused
cemetery, kneels beside a certain grave, arises,
lights his pipe, sits down on the mound and in-
dulges in a “quiet smoke and think.” After a
quarter of an hour or so, he knocks the ashes out,
of his pipe on the footstone, gets up, and saunters
back whence he came.

Rev. J. W. Simmons, for many years an
editorial contributor to the Hobart Mercury,
says, “it is very difficult to find an Austraban
colonial paper in any public library in England.
The Sydney Mail is forwarded to London Guild
hall, but the librarian has no place to show it.’
A souvenir of his trip is a copy of a bill to provide
pensions for the aged, which passed the Commons
in 1772, but was thrown out by the Lords.

The reporters for a certain ponderou B '
daily newspaper in a certain Australian Parlia-
meut should perform their famous “ suppression ’’
act with more judgment. The other evening,,
right through an able and blistering speech which*
made their party squirm, the reporter who was*
taking the “notes” was incessantly told in an*
Irish whisper bj*- his superior, a partisan scribe,
“ don’t take that down !”
In New South Wales, it appears, things
are now so arranged that a woman can no longer
dump down anybody’s name, in a loose and
general sort of way, as the father of an illegitimate'
infant—in fact, the father isn’t put down at all.
This is a remarkably good thing for the father'
and possibly for other fathers as well. It has*
often occurred to us how anybody could go for'
his pious enemy by getting consignments of
Evening News babies at intervals, and register-
ing them as the illegitimate offspring of—well,,
say a bishop for instance. He could enter a
dozen infants to the good man’s credit in different-
districts round about his diocese, and then*
simply leave the encumbrances on doorsteps, and 1
lie low till the blasting fact came out. It would'
come out to a dead certainty, and when the*
sufferer found himself put down in all directions*
as the father of Mary Jones’s child or Jane-
Smith’s twins, and the imaginary Jones andl
Smith were both undiscoverable, and the event
was registered as taking place in some lodging
house —since pulled down —in a crowded slum, in*
the presence of Rebecca Jenkins—who had re-
moved to parts unknown, whau could he do?
Just about nothing.
The World’s Fair.
Few persons among the population of Victoria*
will, we premise, hold with Mr. Munro’s tacit
refusal to benefit the colony by arranging for the-
exhibits of its products in the great World’s Fair
to be held in Chicago early in 1893. We badly
require the extension of the comparatively small
exchange of products now carried on between
Victorian markets particularly, and the markets
of the western slope of the American continent..
To this end, if the exhibit of this country’s goods,
in the World's Fair jrere authorised, much goodl
would accrue. At present, the Premier’s action
resembles the procedure of the hopelessly foolish
Conservative merchant who refuses to advertise*
his wares at all, when his policy should be exactly
the reverse. For ourselves we have not the*
slightest intention of taking down our sign by
curtailing our advertising. This, or any other of:
the forms of advertising we indulge in is not
intended for the people who have already used our
medicines. Every bottle sold is the best
advertisement the purchaser could have, and in
nine cases out of ten he has told his neighbour..
But in the daily increase of our population this,
writing is for the man or woman who has not yet
used or heard of our remedies. Once an article
or a pamphlet issued by us reaches the person for
whom it is intended, or to whom it is directed 1 ,,
the missive has no doubt made its mark, but in-
directly the advertisement goes on forever so to*
speak, or the ball is kept rolling by the in-
formation which passes from neighbour to*
neighbour or from friend to friend. They alll
know when they get hold of a good medicine, andl
a good thing bears being talked about. As a>.
matter of fact wo deliver either by post or
messenger a copy of our annual pamphlet to every
house to be found in all these Australasian
colonies and New Zealand, and this is additional!
to all the other systems of giving publicity to our
wares we adopt. These advertisements all allude*
in some form or other to the peculiar cir-
cumstances that in the possession of the patent;
for Warner’s safe cure we are honored to become*
the means, if they will only accept it, of the*
restoration to normal health of every man or
woman suffering from the effects of kidney or
liver disease, if Warner’s safe remedies are taken
by them as directed. We are happy to know
that our. various remedies are rapidly becoming as
well-known as were treacle and brimstone in the
days of our boyhood, and that, becoming the
household medicine, they receive in many houses
in the country the place of honor near the kitchen
clock.

QUEENSLAND.
NOTICE.
Sale by Auction of Crown Lands in Large
Portions.
IT is notified for public information that, under
the provisions of “ The Special Sales of Land
Act of 1891,” Country Lands in the Colony of
Queensland, distant not less than twenty miles
from a navigable river or from a railway, or from
the surveyed line of a railway for the construction
of which money has been raised by loan, may be
offered for Sale by Auction, in portions not exceed-
ing 5120 acres. The upset price will in no case be
less than 10s. per acre.
The time for payment may be extended to a
period not exceeding three years, instalments
payable later than six months from the date of
sale bearing interest at 5 per cent, per annum.
Persons desirous of having Crown lands offered
at auction are invited to apply to the Under
Secretary for Public Lands, Brisbane, or any
Crown Lands Commissioner.
A. S. COWLEY,
Secretary for Public Lands.
Department of Public Lands,
Brisbane, 28th October, 1891.
